Partie II – Chapitre 4

Cluster 1: Planned technology-driven ISSE. The ecosystems in this cluster are mainly planned

(74%), usually by the government in a top-down approach. These ecosystems are supported by

large-scale investments, mainly in infrastructure, which may also explain their longevity as it is

more  difficult  to  backtrack  when  a  lot  of  money,  time  and  energy  have  been  invested  in  the

process, even when results are not forthcoming. These ecosystems are frequently managed by

the  government  or  in  the  framework  of  specific  projects,  but  their  internal  governance  is  not

explored in the papers included in our literature review. However, a common feature is the weak

involvement  of  end  users  in  the  planning,  implementation  and  functioning  of  the  ecosystem,

suggesting their governance is rather centralised.

Many  agropoles  fall  into  this  cluster,  reflecting  the  belief  that  innovation  can  be  fostered  by

clustering  organisations  in  the  same  location  or  acting  as  intermediaries  between  service

providers  and  innovators.  The  primary  mode  of  support  is  the  provision  of  services,  which  is

overrepresented (42%). The worldview of these ecosystems is largely technology-oriented (53%),

although  there  are  instances  of  sustainability-oriented,  business-oriented,  and  systemic

perspectives.

All ISSEs in this cluster pursue a mission, with a focus on the diffusion of research innovations, on

enhancing competitiveness and value creation. They operate and have an impact at a regional

scale.  Academic  research  on  the  performance  of  ISSEs  in  this  cluster  remains  limited  and

inconclusive, with existing studies presenting contradictory findings regarding their success.

Cluster 2: University and research-driven service ecosystems for participatory innovation.

This cluster is characterised by a worldview centred on inclusivity of diverse stakeholders as a key

driver of innovation. It is based on the desire to create a stronger link between scientific research,

training and bringing innovations to market (particularly through entrepreneurship). The approach

of  innovation  is  deliberately  in  opposition  to  the  linear  model  of  research,  where  innovations

developed  in  laboratories  or  experimental  stations  were  later  disseminated  to  farmers  for

implementation. This can take two different forms: either in a model where universities foster the

development  of  innovation  through  entrepreneurial  incubation  (linking  students,  innovative

projects and the private sector); or in participatory research and innovation, where research acts

as a broker in complex, multi-actor innovation processes.

The emergence of these systems is described as intermediate or ad hoc, reflecting the dual role

of universities – local actors invested in the development of their territories but also influenced by

state directives. Unlike many innovation ecosystems, these clusters are not necessarily mission-

146

Partie II – Chapitre 4

oriented: only 21% have a clear mission-driven agenda, and when they are, their mission is often

focused on agroecological transitions. These ecosystems operate mainly at regional scale and

are  notably  over-represented  in  Europe  (42%),  compared  to  the  general  population  of  articles

included in the literature review (31%).

Cluster  3:  Mission-Driven  Co-Innovation  Platforms.  This  cluster  primarily  consists  of

innovation  platforms  and  living  labs,  which  are  characterised  by  a  strong  emphasis  on  the

inclusion of diverse actors in innovation processes and support mechanisms. This inclusivity is

reflected in the type of support they provide, which focuses on participation and co-innovation,

as well as the importance of establishing dedicated spaces to promote networking.

This  cluster  is  well  represented  in  Africa  (48%),  where  these  coordination  models  have  been

strongly  promoted  and  supported  by  research  and  development  projects.  This  influence  is

obvious  considering  the  actors  who  create  the  platforms  –  68%  originate  from  projects  –  and

considering those who manage them, primarily research projects or research actors.

The emergence of these ecosystems is largely considered to be intermediate: while not directly

initiated by service providers, they are driven by actors with a good knowledge of local realities

who strive to include local stakeholders as far as possible. Governance structures vary across the

ecosystems  in  this  cluster,  ranging  from  fully  shared  to  intermediate  forms.  End  users  are

generally  well  integrated  in  the  ecosystem,  or  at  least  the  systems  strive  to  ensure  their

involvement. All the ecosystems in this cluster are mission-oriented, with two primary missions

emerging:  improving  the  productivity  of  a  specific  sector  and  facilitating  the  agroecological

transition. Accordingly, these ecosystems tend to operate at local to regional scale.

These ecosystems are generally less durable than those in Cluster 1, which is directly linked to

their mission. Actors in these ecosystems come together to tackle a specific problem, and once

that mission is accomplished, the ecosystem often dissolves, although it may reconfigure with

different actors and a new mission. This results in a structure that is comparatively more flexible

and prone to reconfiguration than Clusters 1 and 2. Performance is not always the focus of the

documents reviewed (36%), but when evaluated, 59% of these ecosystems are successful.

Cluster 4: Regional innovator-led ecosystem. Cluster 4, along with the two remaining clusters,

is more marginal in the literature reviewed, as it contains only five articles. The conclusions drawn

from  this  cluster  are  therefore  general  and  should  be  interpreted  with  caution.  This  cluster

describes  ecosystems  that  have  emerged  in  an  ad-hoc  manner,  initiated  by  innovators  and

centred  on  them.  Unlike  previous  clusters,  which  focus  on  service  providers  who  support

innovation by offering services to innovators – who are often described rather vaguely – this cluster

147

Partie II – Chapitre 4

places  innovators  at  its  core.  It  examines  the  network  of  support  services  providers  (SSPs)  to

whom they have access. These SSPs include the other farmer-innovators with whom the innovator

is  networking  and  who  serve  as  key  sources  of  information.  The  primary  form  of  support

highlighted in this cluster is the provision and exchange of services.

The ecosystems in this cluster are characterised by a strong desire for inclusivity, with high to very

high  involvement  of  end-users,  in  line  with  the  focus  on  the  ecosystem  on  the  innovator.  The

literature on this cluster is particularly interested in the structure of these ecosystems and the

challenges they face. Governance tends to be shared, with no central or pivotal actor in some

cases,  while  in  others,  a  farmer,  an  NGO,  or  an  ad-hoc  committee  plays  that  role.  These

ecosystems  primarily  have  regional  roots  and  typically  a  relatively  large  number  of  members,

although relationships between them are not necessarily close or regular. They are not necessarily

mission-oriented;  the  actors  do  not  get  together  to  achieve  a  common  goal,  but  rather  to

exchange knowledge, share expertise, and access services.

Most of these ecosystems are described in Europe (60%), which raises the question of whether

these ecosystems are more prevalent in Europe or whether there is a research bias related to the

way the relationship between innovators and their accompanying ecosystem is studied. In terms

of  performance,  these  ecosystems  are  either  not  evaluated  at  all  or  are  considered  to

underperform (in three out of five cases). This is attributed to difficulties in integrating relevant

actors, operational challenges, and to a rapid decline in functionality.

Cluster 5: Centralised state-led ecosystems. This cluster represents an extreme and somewhat

exaggerated version of Cluster 1, primarily consisting of agropoles whose emergence was tightly

scheduled and whose governance is extremely centralised. In this model, end-user involvement

is  minimal. Like  in  Cluster  1,  the  emergence  of these  ecosystems  is  planned  by  the  state  and

generally  accompanied  by  substantial  investment  in  infrastructure.  The  support  model  in  this

cluster  revolves  around  the  provision  of  services  to  innovative  farmers  and/or  acting  as

intermediary in their access to services.

The worldview underpinning the actors driving this cluster is business oriented, as reflected in one

of its primary missions: improving the competitiveness of agricultural production on both national

and  international  markets.  The  other  key  mission,  promoted  by  governments,  is  national  food

security which, in this context, is interpreted as large-scale, low-cost production aimed at feeding

a growing, impoverished population. The articles in this cluster focus on the performance of these

ecosystems  and  conclude  that  they  largely  underperform,  failing  to  provide  the  necessary

services to innovators and falling short of achieving their food security goals.

148

Partie II – Chapitre 4

Cluster 6: Unclassifiable articles that fail to identify ISSE case studies. This cluster does not

represent a specific type of ecosystem but rather contains more general articles that do not focus

on a specific case study. Most key factors cannot even be inferred; thus, we went no further in the

analysis of this cluster. However, we decided to keep the articles in this cluster because of their

relevant inputs for a general understanding of the situation of ISSE in agriculture.

5

DISCUSSION

5.1

Innovation support services ecosystems in the particular context of Sub-Saharan

Africa and gaps in the literature

This study aimed at enlightening the situation of ISSEs in Sub-Saharan Africa (SSA) as a first step

towards supporting these initiatives. Thus, in this section, we focus on the ISSEs present in SSA.

All  the  clusters  identified  in  our  results  are  present  in  SSA,  although  their  distribution  differs

slightly from the  global  average.  In  SSA,  62%  of  Innovation  Support  Service  Ecosystems  (ISSE)

belong to Cluster 3, 15% are linked to universities (cluster 2), 9% to cluster 1, and 6% to cluster 5.

Notably,  the  two  state-linked  agropoles  (cluster  1  and  5)  account  for  15%  of  ISSEs  in  SSA,

compared to 24% in the general articles included in our review. This is in line with the findings of

(Audouin, Raharison, et al., 2021) concerning the composition of ISS providers in Madagascar,

where  services  are  mainly  provided  by  public

institutions  (research  and  development

organisations), farmer organisations, development NGOs and projects, showing less government

control on service provision compared to other countries.

The cases of Clusters 2 and 3 are particularly interesting because they are both led and described

by  academic  or  academically  adjacent  actors,  i.e.  universities,  and  research  or  development

projects.  Given  the  researchers’  easier  access  to  field  data  on  initiatives  in  which  they  have

participated, we wonder if there is a representation bias leading to an overrepresentation in the

literature of these ISSEs compared to their actual prevalence on the ground.

What  is  more,  our  analysis  revealed  extreme  cases  across  the  governance  and  emergence

planning spectrums. Cluster 5, which is both highly centralised and tightly planned, was found to

be inefficient, while Cluster 4, which is very horizontal and emerged in an ad-hoc manner, was

also found to be inefficient. The latter result contradicts the findings of Thorgren et al. (2009, p.

148)  that ‘bottom-up formed networks achieve greater  innovative performance’. This raises the

question of whether performance is mostly to be found in the intermediate structures. In terms of

emergence  and  governance,  these  intermediate  structures  could  be  orchestrated  by  a  hub

149

Partie II – Chapitre 4

organisation  that  does  not  function  in  the  same  way  as  entirely  self-organising  networks  but

whose structure is less centralised and vertical than Cluster 5 (and to a lesser extent, Cluster 1).

This hypothesis aligns with the literature on business ecosystems, suggesting that ecosystems

led  by  an  orchestrating  firm  practising  effective  coordination  achieve  lower  transaction  costs

(Foss  et  al.,  2023)  and  greater  alignment  around  a  shared  value  proposition  (Jacobides  et  al.,

2018).

Our review not only revealed the cases documented in the literature but also identified a gap  –

specifically, a missing cluster we had expected to find – a type of ISSE which emerges ad-hoc yet

has intermediate governance. However, this scenario was described in one of the documents we

reviewed  (Toillier  &  Kola,  2020),  suggesting  that  it  is  a  real-world  phenomenon  that  is

underrepresented in the literature. Due to the way statistical treatments such as MCA and AHC

were  conducted,  this  case  was  integrated  in  another  cluster,  but  we  believe  it  is  important  to

acknowledge the possible existence of this particular type of ISSE for several reasons. First, ad-

hoc ISSEs appear to be those where, due to their less rigid structure, capacity-building efforts may

be  the  most  effective.  Additionally,  the  types  of  support  provided  to  innovators  in  these

ecosystems tend to be more tailored and less standardised, and our results tend to show that in

that case, support may be more effective. As such, this model could be one that public authorities

and  donors  should  prioritise  for  development  support.  Additionally,  although  agropoles  are

currently  gaining  popularity  in  Africa,  the  model  of  planned  ISSEs  is  much  less  replicable,

particularly in terms of the investment required.

5.2

Definition of the new concept of innovation support services ecosystems

Our  review  highlights  the  very  fragmented  understanding  of  service  delivery  to  agrifood

innovators,  and  focussed  descriptions  on  the  leading  roles  of  traditional  organizations  in

agricultural innovation (government, research, innovators) while gaps remain on some innovation

models, coordination modes, or actors (such as the private sector) which are totally overlooked.

Therefore, we propose a definition of ISSE that better grasp the potential diversity of ISS providers,

and  their  goals,  while  keeping  the  core  commonalities of  service ecosystems described in  the

literature. The definition is as follows: “a network of interconnected organisations within a specific

geographic scale that, over a certain period of time, provide needs-based services to innovators

in  the  relevant  field  toward  the  achievement  of  a  societal  mission  with  sustainable  positive

impacts on the agrifood systems.” This definition highlights four key elements (see Figure 19).

150

Partie II – Chapitre 4

Figure  19:  Literature-based  model  of  an  agricultural  Innovation  Support  Services  Ecosystem
(ISSE)

First,  ISSEs  consist  of  organisations  which  are  interlinked.  To  be  considered  part  of  the

ecosystem,  an organisation  must have connections with at least one other  organisation in the

network. According to Jacobides et al. (2018, p. 2255), the term ‘ecosystem’ refers to “interacting

organisations, enabled by modularity, not hierarchically managed.” These connections can vary

greatly in nature, intensity, and frequency, and include activities such as exchanging information,

conducting joint activities, or the training members of one organisation by another.

Second, ISSEs are almost  always rooted in a specific geographic scale. This includes both the

location  of  ecosystem  members  and  the  geographical  area  where  the  ecosystem  operates,

particularly  where  the  innovators  who  benefit  from  the  available  services  are  located.  The

geographic scope is a distinguishing factor among ecosystems: while most operate at the regional

level,  some  function  more  locally,  and  others  have  national  or  even  sub-regional  reach.  This

geographic scale not only influences the proximity of ecosystem members to each other and to

the innovators  they  support,  but also allows access to  expertise and services that may not be

available at a more local level. In contrast to the Agricultural Innovation System (AIS), which is

defined  at  national  level  and  encompasses  all  advisory  systems  that  support  innovation  in  a

country, the ecosystems of support services we observed are much smaller in scale, more closely

aligned  with  micro-AKIS  (Labarthe  et  al.,  2018)  or Innovation  sub-systems  of  AIS  (Mathé  et  al.

151

Partie II – Chapitre 4

2022).  We  believe  that  the  ecosystem-level  could  allow  to  propose  a  more  managerial

perspective, focusing on how organisations coordinate, how joint strategies are defined, and how

collective decisions are made at the inter-organisational level.

Third,  ISSEs  behave  as  organisations  that  provide  services  to  innovators.  As  defined  in  the

literature, these services (ISS) can vary significantly (Faure et al. 2019; Mathé et al. 2019). They

may be offered at different stages of the innovation process, depending on the innovators’ support

needs, from the original idea and planning to development, implementation, dissemination, and

embedding (Wielinga, 2016).

Fourth, ISSEs operate within a specific innovation domain. These ecosystems are predominantly

mission-oriented  (74%),  i.e.  in  line  with  Shaikh  &  Levina's  (2019)  claim  that  an  ecosystem’s

mission is a key attribute of its strategic governance. Ecosystems which are not mission-oriented

can be  divided  into  two  types:  those that  have  impacts  at  a  large  scale  –  making  it difficult  to

identify a single mission – and those whose only goal is linking stakeholders or organisations to

improve  performances  but  with  no  specific  goal  mentioned.  Our  study  reveals  that  service

ecosystems supporting agricultural innovation predominantly focus on improving quality of life or

enhancing business opportunities, aligning with the societal mission outlined in Mission-Oriented

Innovation  Systems  (Hekkert  et  al.,  2020).  Beyond  such  specific  missions,  ISSEs  are  tied  to

specific domains of agricultural innovation, for example, digital agriculture, agroecology, or agro-

food processing.

5.3

Contributions, limits and future research

This  research  makes  several  key  contributions  to  the  literature.  First,  we  identified  that  the

literature partially describes the  ISSE phenomenon, with  different frameworks that differ about

emergence dynamics, governance model, worldview, and the support mechanisms provided by

their  members.  Some  commonalities  are  identified:  the  level  of  actors’  proximity,  the  distinct

roles of researchers, state and innovators and the key roles of the organisation who orchestrates

the  ecosystem.  However,  from  our  point  of  view,  based  on  empirical  observations,  key

characteristics of ISSE are missing such as the existence of past ties between members of the

ecosystem, the rules and regulations established, the nature of the mission and an evaluation of

the performance of the ecosystem. Therefore, we proposed a definition of ISSE that might help to

better grasp the ISSE phenomenon, and to have a deeper look at some crucial aspect of service

delivery in the context of agrifood innovation support.

152

Partie II – Chapitre 4

Second,  the  study  examines  the  specific  context  of  Sub-Saharan  Africa,  where  the  Grand

Challenges  are  particularly  acute.  It  reveals  which  ISSEs  are  present,  and  which  are

underrepresented in the literature. Further, it identifies which ISSEs perform best and have the

most potential to tackle Grand Challenges in Africa.

However,  the  study  also  has  limitations.  The  articles  we  reviewed  were  not  written  to  directly

address our specific research question. Consequently, available data did not enable us to draw

conclusions on all relevant aspects, such as the presence of past ties, the functioning of internal

governance,  the  rules  of  conduct  and  sanction  mechanisms  or  the  roles  played  by  the  hub

organisation. Future research could address these questions through dedicated data collection.

Moreover, certain situations, particularly those involving ad hoc ISSEs in Sub-Saharan Africa that

are  orchestrated  with  the  goal  of  contributing  to  the  agroecological  transition,  are  either

underexplored  or  not  described  in  sufficient  detail.  This  gap  presents  opportunities  for  further

research.

Finally,  these  ISSEs  exhibit  characteristics  of  Open  Social  Innovation  (OSI)  as  defined  by

Chesbrough  &  Di  Minin  (2014),  i.e.  pursue  both  economic  and  social  objectives.  In  future

research, it may be useful to further explore the implications of this dual focus on the functioning

of the ecosystem.

6

CONCLUSION

Our  research  work  is  based  on  the  view  that  looking  at  innovation  support through  the  lens  of

innovation  support  services  ecosystems  offer  promising  perspectives  to  improve  enabling

environment of agrifood innovations aiming at addressing the Grand challenges in the agricultural

sector. To better understand how Innovation Support Services providers work and collaborate, the

aim  of  this  paper  was  to  characterise  the  diversity  and  efficiency  of  service  ecosystems  that

support agrifood innovation, by detailing their main characteristics. To answer these questions,

we built a methodology to carry out a literature review on a concept that has not yet appeared in

the literature. Multivariate analyses made it possible to identify five clusters of interest (the sixth

cluster  contained  general  articles  which  did  not  allow  us  to  clearly  define  the  ISSE).  The  six

clusters pertained to: i) planned ISSE for development and promotion of technological solutions;

ii)  university-research  led  ISSE  for  participatory  research;  iii)  ISSE  promoting  interaction  and

access to services for co-innovation; iv) regional innovator-led ISSE; v) long-lasting government-

led  ISSE  with highly  centralised  governance  and vi)  unclassifiable  articles  that did not  make  it

153

Partie II – Chapitre 4

possible to identify ISSE case-studies. These clusters of ISSEs can be distinguished based on two

main criteria: the way they emerged, and their ongoing governance. Our analysis also underlined

the  importance of the  missions  of  the  ISSEs  usually  related  to  societal  or  social  problematics

identified in the country or region of the ISSE and lack of information regarding their performance

to fulfil them.

These results allow us to define the new concept of Innovation Support Services Ecosystems as

“a network of interconnected organisations within a specific geographic scale that, over a certain

period  of  time,  provide  needs-based  services  to  innovators  in  the  relevant  field  toward  the

achievement of a societal mission with sustainable positive impacts on the agrifood systems”.

Further, we were able to characterise the low research interest for the ISSEs in Africa, with focus

on  short-term  mechanism  (project-based  platforms)  rather  than  long-term  approaches  of

enabling environments around innovators.

While the results we obtained are already of considerable interest, the descriptions of some of

the ecosystems are incomplete and primary data need to be collected to deepen knowledge on

their emergence and functioning to identify levers to support their further development.

154

Partie II

CONCLUSION PARTIE II

La  Partie  II,  composée  d’un  chapitre  de  résultats  et  d’un  article,  a  permis  d’approfondir  notre

compréhension des Écosystèmes de Services Supports à l’Innovation (ESSI). À partir d’une revue

de littérature, nous avons défini ce concept, identifié les éléments qui unifient ces écosystèmes

tout  en  rendant  compte  de  leur  diversité,  et  mis  en  lumière  leurs  spécificités  par  rapport  aux

écosystèmes  de  services  présents  dans  d’autres  secteurs  de  l’innovation  sociale.  L’analyse  a

également  souligné  les  particularités  du  secteur  agricole  en  matière  d’accompagnement  des

transitions, en révélant la diversité des configurations existantes. Cela a conduit à une définition

contextualisée des ESSI dans le secteur agricole et agroalimentaire, fondée sur deux critères de

différenciation principaux : leurs modalités d’émergence et leur mode de gouvernance.

Les informations disponibles dans la littérature sont parcellaires concernant la phase cruciale de

l’émergence, notamment concernant les écosystèmes qui se forment de manière ad-hoc pour

accompagner des innovations en agroécologie. La Partie III vise à approfondir ces connaissances

grâce  aux  deux  articles  présentés  dans  les  Chapitre  5  :  et  Chapitre  6  :.  Ces  deux  articles

s’appuient  sur  des  données  primaires  collectées  grâce  à  des  entretiens  semi-directifs  dont  le

guide d’entretien est disponible en Annexe C :.

PARTIE III. L’EMERGENCE

Partie III – Chapitre 5

Chapitre 5 :  Orchestrer la diversité :
aligner les organisations qui
accompagnent les innovations
sociales pour une agriculture durable
en Afrique subsaharienne

157

Partie III – Chapitre 5

Résumé étendu

MOTIVATION

L’émergence  constitue  une  étape  décisive  du  cycle  de  vie  d’un  écosystème.  De  nombreux

écosystèmes échouent à se structurer durablement ou disparaissent dès cette phase, qui s’avère

également  déterminante  pour  la  qualité  des  relations  interorganisationnelles  à  venir.  Mieux

comprendre  cette  phase  apparaît  donc  essentiel,  afin  de  pouvoir  mieux  l’accompagner  et

favoriser à la fois la pérennisation des écosystèmes et la solidité des dynamiques relationnelles

qui s’y instaurent.

OBJECTIF

Cet  article  vise  à  comprendre  les  conditions  d’émergence  des  ESSI  en  identifiant  les  facteurs

contraignants, internes et externes, que l’organisation hub doit lever afin de favoriser l’alignement

des organisations et rendre l’émergence possible.

POSITIONNEMENT DU CHAPITRE DANS LA PROBLEMATIQUE D’ENSEMBLE

Cet  article  se  place  donc  à  l’échelle  de  l’écosystème  pour  analyser,  dans  une  perspective

processuelle,  la  manière  dont  les  relations  se  mettent  en  place  et  dont  les  organisations

s’alignent  pour  permettre  l’émergence  de  l’écosystème.  La  Figure  20  ci-dessous  illustre  son

positionnement dans le design général de nos travaux de thèse.

158

Partie III – Chapitre 5

Figure 20: Positionnement de l'article 2 dans le design de la thèse

RESUME (EN FRANÇAIS)

Des  écosystèmes  bien  organisés  de  services  support  à  l’innovation  apparaissent  comme

essentiels pour accélérer les innovations dans le secteur agricole et contribuer à répondre aux

grands défis liés à l’atteinte des Objectifs de Développement Durable. Un approfondissement de

notre compréhension des facteurs qui favorisent l’émergence de ces écosystèmes est nécessaire

pour  en faciliter  le déploiement. Cette étude mobilise le cadre théorique des écosystèmes de

services, avec une attention particulière portée aux dynamiques d’alignement organisationnel et

d’émergence des écosystèmes. Elle propose une lecture intégrée des modalités de déploiement

des  services  d’appui  à  l’innovation  agricole  à  travers  l’évolution  des  coordinations  et  des

arrangements institutionnels. En tenant compte des spécificités de l’innovation dans le secteur

agricole, nous proposons un éclairage nouveau sur les conditions d’émergence des écosystèmes

de services, en soulignant le rôle clé de l’organisation hub qui permet de surmonter les facteurs

contraignants de l’alignement des organisations pour la cocréation de valeur.

Nous avons adopté une approche par étude de cas, reposant sur des entretiens semi-directifs et

sur  plus  de  cinq  années  de  données  empiriques  concernant  des  dispositifs  innovants  de

labellisation de l’agriculture biologique en Afrique subsaharienne. Les Systèmes Participatifs de

Garantie  offrent  une  opportunité  de  certification  biologique  ou  agroécologique  aux  petits

producteurs  pour  les  marchés  nationaux.  Il  s’agit  d’une  innovation  systémique,  nécessitant  la

mise en œuvre conjointe d’innovations techniques, sociales et organisationnelles, et mobilisant

plusieurs services de soutien à l’innovation.

Une  analyse  processuelle  par  croisement  temporel  (temporal  bracketting),  nous  a  permis

d’identifier  trois  phases  dans  l’émergence  de  ces  écosystèmes  :  :  les  étapes  préliminaires,  la

naissance et la croissance. Nous distinguons les facteurs contraignants internes à l’écosystème

et à son fonctionnement, des facteurs externes liés au contexte. Ce travail contribue également

à  enrichir  la  théorie  de  l’alignement,  en  soulignant  sa  pertinence  dans  les  contextes  du  Sud

global,  et  en  identifiant  deux  nouveaux  facteurs  contraignants  à  l’alignement  :  les  projets  de

développement  internationaux  et  la  demande  des  utilisateurs  finaux.  Il  met  en  lumière  le  rôle

crucial de l’organisation hub dans le dépassement de ces contraintes.

160

Partie III – Chapitre 5

ABSTRACT (EN ANGLAIS)

Well  organised  ecosystems  of  Innovation  Support  Services  appear  critical  to  accelerate

innovations in agriculture and contribute to responding to the Grand Challenges for achieving the

SDGs. Deepening our understanding of the drivers of service ecosystems’ emergence is needed

to  facilitate  their  deployment.  This  study  mobilizes  the  service  ecosystems  framework  with

particular  attention  to  the  dynamics  of  organisational  alignment  and  ecosystem  emergence,

offering an integrated perspective on how agricultural innovation support services are deployed

through  evolving  coordination  and  institutional  arrangements.  Considering  the  specificities  of

innovation in the agricultural sector, we propose new insights on service ecosystems’ emergence

through the role of the hub organisation in overcoming the constraining factors of organisations’

alignment for value co-creation.

We adopted a case study approach based on semi-structured interviews and over 5 years of case

study data regarding innovative labelling of organic farming in Sub-Saharan Africa. Participatory

Guarantee Systems offer an opportunity for organic or agroecological certification to small-scale

farmers for national markets. It is a systemic innovation requiring a diversity of innovations to be

implemented:  technical,  social,  and  organizational

innovations;  and  mobilizing  several

innovation support services.

A  processual  analysis  through  temporal  bracketing  allowed  us  to  identify  three  phases  of

emergence: preliminary stages, birth and growth. We differentiated between constraining factors

internal  to  the  ecosystem  and  its  functioning  and  external  factors  depending  on  contextual

elements. We also contributed to enriching the theory of alignment and its relevance in the Global

South context by adding two new constraining factors to alignment: international development

projects and demand of end-users and underlying the crucial role of the hub organisation.

161

Partie III – Chapitre 5

Article

1

INTRODUCTION

The  world,  particularly  countries  in  the  Global  South,  grapple  with  what  scholars’  term  Grand

Challenges  –  complex  but  identifiable  problems  profoundly  affecting  a  large  number  of

individuals (Eisenhardt et al., 2016; Ferraro et al., 2015; George et al., 2016). Solutions to these

challenges are composite and are likely to require interdisciplinary approaches and coordinated

efforts. The agricultural sector intersects with several Grand Challenges, including food security,

poverty  alleviation,  climate  change  mitigation,  and  addressing  negative  consequences  of  the

Green Revolution. The necessity of an ecological transition is largely shared between actors, and

international donors advocate and finance initiatives towards this goal.

In this process, organic labels are seen as one of the means able to pull a variety of innovations

contributing  to  the  transformation  of  food  systems  toward  more  sustainability.  Participatory

Guarantee Systems are national labels allowing to certify agricultural products for local markets,

based on a standard defined collectively by local stakeholders (producers, retailers, consumers,

etc.).  This  kind  of  organic  label  is  a  systemic  innovation  requiring  several  changes  in  the

agricultural production systems (new farming practices, new bio-inputs, new land management

methods), in  the farm functioning,  in  organisational  structuration of supply  chains  (creation of

cooperatives,  associations,  implementation  of  certification  mechanisms,  new  marketing  and

trading modes), and new financing modalities. Innovations are thus both technological and socio-

organisational.  All  these

innovations  require  support  to  facilitate  their  emergence  and

acceleration. This support takes the form of Innovation Support Services as varied as knowledge

transfer, intermediation, access to resources, etc. (Faure et al., 2019; Mathé et al., 2016; Toillier

et  al.,  2021).  However,  systemic  innovations  for  agroecological  transitions  require  specific

support due to their multi-dimensional and multi-sectoral nature, involving diverse stakeholders

with  potentially  divergent  interests.  In  the  case  of  institutional  innovations  like  Participatory

Guarantee Systems (PGS), actors such as private companies, consumer associations, producer

groups,  and  NGOs  must  negotiate  compromises  to  align  their  objectives  (financial  benefits,

cheap and quality food, decent living standard). This process often involves external facilitation

to mediate conflicts and support collaboration (Sartas et al., 2020), although in some contexts –

such as in parts of Latin America – PGS have also emerged endogenously..

162

Partie III – Chapitre 5

Structural  adjustment  programmes  in  the  Global  South  during  the  1980s-1990s  led  to  cuts  in

public  agricultural  advisory  services  and  their  privatisation,  prioritising  profitable  sectors  and

export value chains (Faure et al., 2014; Kidd et al., 2000; Klerkx et al., 2006; Labarthe, 2005). This

fragmented  landscape  of  actors  risks  creating  uncoordinated  interventions  that  may  hinder

agroecological transition (Ndah et al., 2025; Toillier et al., 2023). Agroecological transitions are

inherently complex, involving multiple stakeholders, disciplines, and challenges (F.-X. Côte et al.,

2019). Effective coordination is crucial for fostering knowledge exchange, sharing best practices,

and efficiently managing resources across various scales (Angeon et al., 2024; Chaparro-Africano

&  Páramo,  2022;  Greenberg  et  al.,  2023).  The  literature  on  agricultural  innovation  support  has

focused mainly on macro- and micro-level approaches (Audouin, Ranaivomanana, et al., 2025;

Faure et al., 2019), neglecting analysis of the dynamics of inter-organisational collaboration at the

meso-level, which is essential if these interactions are to be strengthened and optimised. In this

paper we propose to study this coordination in the form of an ecosystem of Innovation Support

Services (ISSE)  (Orbell, Toillier, Mignon, et al., 2024) and specifically its emergence, to answer to

the development aid stakeholders’ lack of knowledge on how to enhance their support through

public action, capacity development and investments.

In addition to addressing field inquiries, our analysis aims to extend literature regarding service

ecosystems. Most research on service ecosystems has focused on secondary or tertiary sectors

and a notable dearth remains in understanding the unique characteristics of service ecosystems

within the agricultural sector, especially in the Global South. Currently, the bulk of research on

ecosystem  emergence  has  predominantly  focused  on  business  ecosystems  and  innovation

ecosystems,

leaving  service  ecosystems  relatively  under-described.  While  coordination

mechanisms  have  been  extensively  studied  in  firm  contexts,  these  mechanisms  are  unknown

within  ecosystem  contexts  (Picaud-Bello  et al.,  2022).  Some few  studies in  the  tourism  sector

investigated  it  but  tourism  ecosystems  typically  exhibit  high  structure  and  formalisation  with

highly planned support, which contrasts with the agricultural sector in the Global South. Here,

support is often decentralized, locally-driven, project-based and adapted ad-hoc to the demands

of  innovators  (Audouin,  Ranaivomanana,  et  al.,  2025;  Faure  et  al.,  2019;  Kilelu  et  al.,  2014).

Consequently,  orchestration  mechanisms  differ  significantly,  highlighting  the  need  to

comprehend how  stakeholders  align  and collaborate towards common objectives.  Preliminary

field  observations  revealed  the  central  role  played  by  a  specific  organisation  in  facilitating

interactions between stakeholders over time (Iyabano et al., 2021; Orbell, Toillier, Mignon, et al.,

2024; Toillier & Kola, 2020). This recurrent pattern led us to conceptualise this actor as a “hub

organisation”.  To  analyse  how  such  an  organisation  may  animate  the  interrelations  between

163

Partie III – Chapitre 5

stakeholders over a certain period of time (several years), we formulated an assumption on the

pioneering role of the hub organisation in building the foundations of the service ecosystem and

triggering  the  commitment  and  coordination  of  other  organizations  around  shared  goals  (i.e.

supporting social innovation and co-creating value). Instead of adapting the theory to the context

of  agriculture  in  countries  of  the  Global  South,  we  took  a  demarcation  perspective  (Djellal  &

Gallouj, 2012) questioning the relevance of the theory in a new context and proposing that the

theory  can  be  enriched  by  identifying  the  differences  observed  in  Southern  countries  and

additional constraining factors to consider.

This  study  builds  on  the  service  ecosystems  framework,  which  emphasises  the  relational  and

institutional dimensions of value co-creation. Within this broader perspective, we foreground two

key but often under-theorised dimensions in the context of ecosystem formation: emergence and

alignment. Emergence draws attention to the evolving nature of roles, coordination mechanisms,

and  institutional  arrangements  over  time.  Alignment  provides a  structural  lens  to  analyse  how

heterogeneous  actors  gradually  converge  around  shared  objectives.  By  articulating  these

dimensions within the service ecosystems approach, we offer a more operational and dynamic

perspective on how innovation ecosystems take shape and are sustained.

Our research question is the following: How does the hub organisation orchestrate the alignment

of organisations, to allow the emergence of a service ecosystem supporting systemic agricultural

innovations in the context of the Global South?

From a theoretical perspective, this paper aims to enrich the theory of alignment in two ways. First

by  describing  the  role  of  the  hub  organisation  in  the  process.  And  second,  by  enriching  the

alignment criteria identified in the literature, to better understand and explain the phenomenon.

To  achieve  this,  it  seeks  to  leverage  existing  research  on  business  and  service  ecosystems  in

sectors beyond  agriculture.  By  doing  so,  it  intends to  deepen  insights  into  service  ecosystems

within the agricultural sector, specifically regarding agroecological transitions. From a managerial

perspective, this paper aims to enhance the understanding of the emergence and functioning of

innovation  support  services  ecosystem  (ISSE)  with  a  view  to  improve  the  design  of  capacity

development interventions by governments and development actors. Additionally, the paper aims

to identify the conditions under which service ecosystems for agriculture emerge in the Global

South and the role played by the hub organisation.

The remainder of the paper is organised as follows: first we will introduce the conceptual building

blocks  of  our  theoretical  framework.  We  begin  by  presenting  how  the  ecosystem  concept  is

appropriate to describe our study object, then develop the concepts of ecosystem emergence,

164

Partie III – Chapitre 5

alignment,  and  orchestration.  In  section  2,  we  will  explain  the  methodology  implemented  to

respond to our research questions. Then we will present our findings and discuss them before

concluding on our theoretical and managerial contributions.

2

THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK

To answer the research question, we will cross-reference different concepts: we will describe our

study object as ecosystems, then rely on the alignment of organisations to study their emergence

and  detail  the  specific  role  of  the  hub  organisation.  Hereafter,  we  will  briefly  present  these

different concepts and how we mobilise them.

As discussed by Daymond et al. (2023), the literature tends to describe ecosystems through two

distinct analytical lenses. One perspective focuses on a shared object or purpose that materially

and  conceptually  unites  participating  organisations  –  such  as

innovation,  a  business

environment, or a technological platform (Jacobides et al., 2018). In such cases, the prefix (e.g.,

"innovation", "platform") refers to the focal object around which actors coordinate. We position

our  analysis  within  this  perspective  and  more  specifically  adopt  the  concept  of  service

ecosystems,  defined  as  dynamic,  self-adjusting  networks  of  autonomous  yet  interdependent

actors  who  co-create  value  through  resource  integration  and  service  exchange  within  shared

institutional  arrangements  (Kutsikos  et  al.,  2014;  Vargo  et  al.,  2020).  This  collaborative  system

relies on institutional rules, norms, and practices to facilitate coordination, innovation, and the

efficient  mobilisation  of  collective  capabilities  .  The  second  perspective,  exemplified  by  the

literature on entrepreneurial ecosystems, focuses less on a shared object and more on a shared

purpose  of  supporting  a  specific  category  of  actors  –  entrepreneurs  –  within  a  geographically

bounded  environment.  Entrepreneurial  ecosystems  are  typically  described  as  "co-located

elements where a variety of actors, functions, and institutions interact to support the creation and

growth  of  new  ventures"  (Thompson  et  al.,  2018).  While  these  perspectives  are  not  mutually

exclusive, they emphasise different organising logics: one object-centric, the other actor-centric.

The  concept  of  innovation  support  service  ecosystems  (ISSEs)  (Orbell,  Toillier,  Mignon,  et  al.,

2024) that we will use refers to a service ecosystem composed of organisations that collaborate

to  deliver  innovation  support  services,  supporting  social  innovations  in the  agricultural  sector.

Thus, it is situated at the intersection of the service and the entrepreneurial ecosystems. From the

first, it draws the idea of organizations coming together to (co-)create a shared object, in this case,

innovation  support  services.  From  the  second,  it  integrates  the  interaction  of  diverse  actors,

165

Partie III – Chapitre 5

functions, and institutions with the overarching aim of fostering entrepreneurship, employment,

value creation, and development.

Emergence  can  be  defined  as  the  creation  process  of  something  new  (organisations,  entities,

structures,  concepts,  etc.  or  here,  an  ecosystem)  from  pre-existing  elements  (here,  the

organisations) where the whole is greater than the sum of its parts (Bhaskar, 2008). In ecosystems

literature, it is crucial to aim attention at this specific phase of the ecosystem life cycle, as it is

compulsory  for  achieving  successful  ecosystems  and  is  a  valuable  way  to  disentangle  the

complexity of service ecosystems (Polese et al., 2020). Furthermore, this phase is often difficult

to  study because of  the high  rate  of failure  of  these  organisational forms, making it  difficult  to

understand the determinants of emergence success or failure and the capabilities mobilised by

the actors (Attour & Barbaroux, 2016). Thomas et al (2022) propose a simple, yet comprehensive

definition  of  service  ecosystem  emergence:  “a  collective  discovery,  sensemaking,  and

negotiation  process  through  which  participants  jointly  discover  and  negotiate  the  elements

necessary  to  establish  a  functioning  ecosystem”.  Various  authors  described  ecosystem

emergence and lifecycle by splitting it in distinct phases. Emergence is not a singular moment but

a continuous process within the ecosystem lifecycle. In this article, we consider emergence as

encompassing all stages preceding the ecosystem's maturity – referred to as "control" by Thomas

(2013) or "stabilisation" by Möller et al. (2020) – before it potentially enters phases of decline, re-

emergence,  or  dormancy  (Cantner  et  al.,  2021;  S.  Michel  &  Defiebre-Muller,  2025).  We  further

examine  this  emergence  phase  by  identifying  and  describing  the  distinct  sub-phases  that

compose it.

The emergence of innovation ecosystems relies on a dynamic interplay between organisational

actions  and  coordination  mechanisms.  For  such  ecosystems  to  emerge,  organisations  must

actively engage; their actions contribute to shaping the ecosystem, which in turn influences their

behaviours. This reciprocal relationship is captured by Taillard et al (2016) through the notion of

actor’s  agency.  Complementing  this  view,  the  structuralist  approach  emphasises  the  need  for

alignment  among  organisations  around  a  shared  value  proposition  (Adner,  2017;  Malherbe  &

Tellier, 2022). Orchestration complements these perspectives by focusing on how one or several

actors enable such alignment by coordinating roles and interactions (Adner, 2017; Autio, 2022).

We  also  take  into  account  external  factors  that can  facilitate  or hinder  coordination,  and thus

influence ecosystem emergence (Malherbe & Tellier, 2023).

Alignment  is  widely  acknowledged  as  a  critical  factor  for  the  success  of  ecosystems.  Prior

research has identified several challenges faced by emerging ecosystems, including the need to

166

Partie III – Chapitre 5

overcome bottlenecks (Hannah & Eisenhardt, 2018), to establish evidence of ecosystem viability

(L. D. W. Thomas & Ritala, 2022), to develop a shared and evolving vision of value , as well as to

meet coordination prerequisites (Jacobides et al., 2018). Nevertheless, Malherbe & Tellier, (2023)

note that the understanding of the factors that enable effective alignment remains incomplete.

Building  on  their  work,  our  conceptual  framework  focuses  on  six  key  alignment  factors:

technology,  innovation,  value  distribution,  identity,  power,  and  public  authorities.  These  six

factors  serve  as  a  foundational  lens  to  analyse  alignment  processes  in  emerging  ecosystems.

While  technology  is  a  key  factor  in  some  contexts,  such  as  the  U.S.  flipper  industry,  it  is  less

relevant  in  the  Global  South’s  agricultural  sector  regarding  the  introduction  of  a  new  label  to

support agroecological transition, where access to new technologies remains limited (Mhlanga &

Ndhlovu, 2023; Smith et al., 2019). Thus, while we retain five of the six original factors, we propose

not to consider technology in our analysis. Innovations relate to the level of innovation and more

importantly  to  co-innovation  in  the  ecosystem  referring  to  the  innovations  needing  other

innovations  to  succeed.  It  is  particularly  crucial due  to  the  interdependence  of  agroecological

innovations,  such  as  certification  labels  and  new  supply  chains.  Value  distribution  in  these

ecosystems  is  shaped  by the  presence of non-profit organisations, extending beyond financial

gains to include intellectual (e.g., certification labels, agricultural input formulas) and social value

(e.g., improved incomes and quality of life for farmers and traders). This emphasis on shared value

(Rubio-Andrés  et  al.,  2022)  is  fundamental  to  how  actors  collaborate,  ensuring  long-term

engagement  and  alignment  around  a  common  purpose.  Identity  mostly  related  to  the  shared

vision, plays a central role in fostering cohesion and legitimacy (L. D. W. Thomas & Ritala, 2022).

While power dynamics can hinder ecosystem  emergence,  they are less prominent in our case

than in competitive business ecosystems. Public authorities also influence alignment through

policy and funding. However, in the Global South, international development projects often play

a particularly structuring role due to the limited capacity of public services. Their strong influence

stems from their financial weight, the orientation of their priorities by international agendas, and

short project  cycles, which  can  create misalignments within the ecosystem  (Alexandre, 2023).

Additionally, this study highlights a constraining factor that is especially salient in this context:

evolving  end-user  demand.  In  our  cases,  this  challenge  is  tied  to  the  implementation  of

Participatory Guarantee Systems (PGS), a form of social innovation developed in the Global South

to certify agroecological products through participatory processes. Each PGS is context-specific,

shaped  by  local  agroecological  and  organisational  characteristics.  However,  as  Chaparro-

Africano & Páramo (2022) show, sustaining these systems is difficult due to limited resources and

167

Partie III – Chapitre 5

the need to mobilise diverse stakeholders, making end-user participation a key yet fragile driver

of ecosystem alignment.

Adner

(2017)  divides  ecosystems

literature

in  two  distinct,  yet  mutually  consistent

conceptualization: (i) ecosystem-as-structure, which he prefers and develops (considering that

the focal value proposition is the most decisive factor to explain alignment) and, (ii) ecosystem-

as-affiliation focusing more on actors who are defined by their network. We will adopt this view,

where  alignment  is  defined  this  way:  “Over  time,  [the  member  organisms]  coevolve  their

capabilities and roles, and tend to align themselves with the direction set by one or more central

companies.”  (Moore,  1996).  The  involvement  of  a  central  actor  to  orchestrate  the  significant

challenges posed by the competitive, collaborative and co-evolutionary aspects of relationships

in an ecosystem (Picaud-Bello et al., 2022), is congruent with both our observations and several

research  streams.  Despite  bearing  different  names  (hub  firm,  catalysing  agent,  focal  firm,

ecosystem  leaders,  keystones,  architects)  (Daymond  et  al.,  2023;  Dhanaraj  &  Parkhe,  2006;

Ekboir & Cruz, 2012; Gulati et al., 2012; Iansiti & Levien, 2004b; Moore, 1993; Valente & Oliver,

2018)  these  organisations  are  described  with the  same  key  objective  to  bring the  ecosystems’

partners “into the positions and roles that its ecosystem strategy envision” (Adner, 2017, p. 47)30.

As Gulati et al. (2012) expressed, it is their attempt to exercise influence over external partners

which leads up to the emergence. We use the term hub organisation to highlight a facilitative, non-

dominating role, particularly suited to contexts in the Global South and to ecosystems primarily

composed of non-profit organisations. These organisations encompass several kinds of roles and

activities: selecting members of the ecosystem, facilitating their interactions, mediating potential

conflicts,  exercising  leadership,  formulating  common  vision  and  missions,  structuring  and

defining members’ roles, facilitating knowledge creation and sharing, representing the ecosystem

towards donors and government, etc. (Adner, 2017; Berthet & Hickey, 2018; Bodin & Crona, 2009;

Bonomi Santos et al., 2023; Favre-Bonté et al., 2016; Wegner et al., 2023). As Valente & Oliver,

(2018)  highlight,  focal  firms  are  essential  to  the  emergence,  acting  as  a  rallying  point  that

facilitates  coordination  and  innovation  between  various  players  to  tackle  the  complexity  of

sustainability  issues  in  sub-Saharan  Africa.  Effective  orchestration  by  such  firms  not  only

enhances collaboration but also reduces transaction costs within ecosystems (Foss et al., 2023;

30 While these terms share a common objective of enabling coordination, they reflect nuanced differences.
A hub firm manages knowledge flows, innovation appropriability, and network stability. A catalysing agent
encourages actors to commit time and resources. A focal firm provides strategic leadership and defines the
ecosystem’s value proposition. Ecosystem leaders leverage their central position to capture greater value.
Keystones  sustain  ecosystem  health  by  offering  stable  and  shared  resources.  Architects  design  and
structure interactions to support long-term ecosystem development.

168

Partie III – Chapitre 5

Shen  et  al.,  2024).  Drawing  a  parallel  with  their  work,  we  will  try  to  understand  how  the  hub

organisation  orchestrates  the  alignment  of  organisations,  to  allow  the  emergence  of  a  service

ecosystem supporting systemic agricultural innovations.

3

METHODOLOGY

To  identify  similar  patterns  in  the  emergence  of  innovation  support  services  ecosystems,  we

conducted a cumulative study based on three case studies depicting the creation of organic or

agroecological PGS (Participatory Guarantee Systems) in three countries of Sub-Saharan Africa

(Burkina Faso, Senegal, and Madagascar). As indicated in the literature, cumulative multiple case

studies are “particularly useful when studying processes” (Garreau, 2020, p. 56) and allow to build

a model of the phenomenon that will be adapted to the context of countries of the global South.

Garreau  (2020, p.  56)  further  specifies that “In  this  context,  2  to  10  cases  are  generally  used”,

which supports the rationale for selecting three case studies in this research.

We  decided  to  have  three  case  studies  because  we  intuitively  thought  that  there  would  be

different characteristics to observe at different stage of the ISSE emergence. Thus, we looked for

cases where the innovation topic is similar (PGS as a new label in the context of each country),

but  where  both  contexts  (different  countries)  and  stages  of  implementation  were  different.

Indeed, the innovation initiative in Burkina Faso and Senegal started several years ago, whereas

in Madagascar implementation of PGS can be considered as still in its infancy allowing to see de

visu,  the  emergence  of  the  ecosystem.  This  cumulative  analysis  will  allow  us  to  observe  the

different stages of the process of emergence. Moreover, we will also retrospective information on

the history of emergence from all case studies (phases one and two are described for all three

case studies while phase three for only two case studies).

3.1

Case studies

In our analysis, we compare three case studies of ecosystems facilitating the implementation of

organic  or  agroecological  Participatory  Guarantee  Systems  (PGS).  PGS  are  defined  as  "locally

focused  quality  assurance  systems.  They  certify  producers  based  on  active  participation  of

stakeholders and are built on a foundation of trust, social networks and knowledge exchange."

(IFOAM,  2008).  Unlike  many  other  service  ecosystems  (in  health  or  energy  sector),  PGS  are

territorialized, fostering proximity and familiarity among actors. Rules and standards are defined

collectively between producers, shop owners, consumers, input sellers, etc.

169

Partie III – Chapitre 5

PGS allow to certify organic or agroecological products for the local market at a lower price than

third-party certification. They generate economic  value  but  also high  social (through  improving

farmers’  income,  their  health,  as  well  as  consumer’s  health),  and  environmental  value  (by

lowering  the  negative  impact  of  agriculture  on  environment).  Based  on  these  shared  values,

implementation of a common vision is easier.

Value  capture  is  distinct  from  business  ecosystems  with  probably  less  competition  between

members  for  value  capture.  PGS  involve  technological  (new  farming  systems),  organizational

(new value chains and new input suppliers for organic inputs), and institutional (creation of the

label)  innovations,  posing  challenges  for  both  evaluation  and  management.  Collaboration  is

essential due to the diverse skills and functions required for success which are almost impossible

to find in a unique actor (Malherbe & Tellier, 2023). We will briefly introduce the three case studies

here (cf Figure 21); a more detailed chronology is available in the appendices (Annexe E :, Annexe

F :, and Annexe G :).

Figure 21: Synthetic presentation of the three cases of Participatory Guarantee Systems studied

The first case study refers to the creation and implementation of the organic PGS label in Burkina

Faso. Initiated by a coalition of NGOs, local associations, and government agencies in the early

2010s,  the  organic  PGS  label  implementation  is  considered  advanced.  It  involves  various

stakeholders  such  as  local  associations  (in  charge  of  the  production,  commercialisation,

technical  support),  farmer  organisations,  supporting  NGOs  (technical  support  and  joint

activities),  projects  and  donors  (technical  and  financial  support),  and  a  private  company  (in

charge  of  the  organic  inputs  supply  and  commercialisation).  The  PGS-standard  was  issued  in

2013 alongside an official law on organic agriculture, with a recognized trademark called BioSPG.

170

Partie III – Chapitre 5

The second case study is the ecosystem of support services supporting the implementation of

the organic PGS in Senegal. Starting in 2015, the support ecosystem for organic PGS in Senegal is

also at an advanced stage, focusing on increasing the number of certified producers under the

BioSenegal  trademark  and  extending  the  geographical  areas  of  implementation.  Led  by  an

umbrella  producers'  organization and  donor  support,  it  includes  local  and international  NGOs

(supporting  production  and  commercialisation),  projects  and  donors  (technical  and  financial

support), private organizations (commercialisation), and farmer  representatives. The first  PGS-

standard was released in 2019, followed by the national organic agriculture law in 2020.

The third case study describes the emerging agro-ecological PGS in Madagascar which started in

2018  and  is  still  in  its  emergent  phase,  led  by  an  umbrella  producers'  organization  and  an

international  NGO.  It  involves  farmer  organizations,  training  centres  (training  and  support  to

production),  an  international  NGO  (financing  and  technical  support),  projects  and  donors

(technical and financial support). A draft version of the PGS-Standard is being tested while the

national  agricultural  organic  law  recognizing  the  concept  of  PGS  was  disclosed  in  2020,  a

trademark exists but remains underutilized. Because it is more recent than the two other PGS, this

case study has been selected to observe at first hand the collaborations that accompanied the

implementation of the ISSE. The first collaborations date back 10-15 years in the Burkina Faso and

Senegal cases. With the case study of  Madagascar, we aim to counterbalance a bias linked to

omissions (voluntary or not) concerning the difficulties encountered, the stakeholders involved,

etc.

3.2

Data collection

Our  data  was  collected  from  several  sources  including  semi-structured  interviews  with  key

stakeholders and secondary data (activity reports, master thesis, websites, documentation from

projects either provided by the stakeholders or through a documentary research) (cf Table 15).

This triangulation both allowed  to reduce the biases of each data source and offers additional

information  of the  social phenomenon we want to describe  (Mathison, 1988).  We chose semi-

structured interviews, a qualitative research method that combines open-ended questions with

a guiding framework, to allow for both consistency across interviews and the flexibility to explore

emergent themes . This approach was particularly suited to our study, as it enabled us to collect

in-depth  insights  on  a  relatively  new  and  underexplored  topic  while  remaining  responsive  to

interviewee perspectives.

171

Partie III – Chapitre 5

Interviews were conducted between May 2022 and December 2023; this extended period of data

gathering is due to our willing to follow the on-going process in the case study in Madagascar by

updating  information  through  a  second  round  of  interviews  with  similar  stakeholders.  The

interview guide was structured in two main themes: first the history and trajectory of emergence

of the ecosystem which supported the implementation of PGS, and second the details of how the

ecosystem  work,  its  governance,  collaboration,  and  competition  existing  between  members,

activities carried out together, etc.

For interviews, both snowball sampling (Eide, 2008) and purposive sampling (Palys, 2008) were

used. Snowball sampling consisted in asking the first respondents the names and details of the

next interesting persons to interview. This method is particularly relevant in our situation because:

(i) these ISSE we never described and no document exists listing the members involved; (ii) we

wanted to interview people who are involved in the same ecosystem and who are in relation with

each other; (iii) we started the interviews with the actor that we thought was the most central in

the  ecosystem  and  supposedly  knowing  all  the  other  actors  of  the  ecosystem.  But,  to

counterbalance  possible  biases  linked  to  omissions  of  actors  involved  (voluntary  or  not),  we

triangulated  this  information  with  purposive  sampling  which  allowed  us  to  identify  from  other

172

Partie III – Chapitre 5

sources  (reports,  websites,  etc.)  additional  relevant  actors  to  interview.  Our  sampling  was  not

meant  to  be  exhaustive but  rather  to  collect  the  vision  of  the  main  actors  and types of  actors

involved  (NGOs,  donors,  civil  society, producer  organisations,  development  projects,  research

institutes, etc.) (see Annexe D :, codes will be referred to, further in the document when verbatim

are cited). However, some organisations were more difficult to reach due to their limited access

and  expertise  in  digital  tools  (some  producer  organisations)  and  due  to  security  reasons,  we

couldn’t  go  to  meet  them  in  person.  We  stopped  the  interviews  when  reaching  theoretical

saturation  which  meant  either  that  we  interviewed  all  the  organisations  of  the  core  of  the

ecosystems (the ones tightly linked to each other), or that no more new information was collected

during interviews.

3.3

Data Analysis

Interviews were fully transcribed and following (Braun & Clarke, 2006) adapted to management

research by Linde et al (2021), we conducted a thematic coding of both the transcribed data and

secondary data collected. Thematic analysis is a valuable approach for uncovering patterns and

connections within large and intricate datasets. Secondary data are mainly issued from “official”

documents (reports, laws, etc.), they were thus mainly used to extract valuable insights on the

chronology of the emergence of the ISSE (as presented in appendices Annexe E :, Annexe F : and

Annexe G :) while interviews allowed to gather information on the functioning of the collaboration,

difficulties encountered, and opinions of stakeholders involved.

We began our analysis with an inductive coding approach to examine how the hub orchestrates

emergence and an abductive coding process to identify the constraining factors of organisational

alignment.  This  involved  integrating  relevant  constraints  previously  identified  by  Malherbe  &

Tellier,  (2023)  while  also  uncovering  two  additional  constraining  factors  through  an  iterative

process of reading, interpreting, and reconceptualising. This investigation allowed us to reveal the

phenomenon of emergence. To further analyse this process, we then applied temporal bracketing

(Langley, 1999) to deconstruct its phases and explore the underlying mechanisms shaping each

stage  (see  data  structure  in  Figure  22Figure  22).  The  analysis  was  facilitated  by  the  Nvivo14

software and allowed  to identify a  total of 151 quotes supporting our analysis and available in

supplementary  documents.  With  the  view  of  limiting  potential  bias  in  thematic  analysis,  we

proceeded  iteratively  because  the  constant  re-examination  of  themes  helped  to  identify

overlooked aspects and to ensure that the themes identified truly reflect the data. Moreover, we

173

Partie III – Chapitre 5

presented  to  some  of  our  interviewees  the  results  of  the  thematic  analysis,  the  chronologies

produced, the phases and constraining factors identified to collect amendments and validation.

Figure 22: Data structure

4

RESULTS

We will here present the main findings of this article: taking a dynamic perspective, we present

the  process  of  emergence  of  Innovation  Support  Services  Ecosystems  (ISSE)  related  to

Participatory  Guarantee  Systems  (PGS).  As  illustrated  in  Figure  24,  this  process  of  emergence

consists  of  switching  from  a  situation  where  the  organisations  are  scattered  and  working

independently  to  a  situation  where  they  are  organised  among  a  service  ecosystem.  This  is

possible  thanks  to  the  orchestration  and  coordination  mechanisms  implemented  by  the  hub

organisation to overcome the constraining factors of organisations’ alignment.

174

E ER E CEP ASESALI   E  ORC ES RA IO     E U OR A ISA IO Coordinationmechanisms oundaryob ects1stordercodesSubthemes hemes Innovations andco innovations Value distribution Identity  co createdvision Power Publicauthorities Internationaldevelopmentprojects Demandfromend users Enhancingmotivation tocollaborate Buildingtrust  osteringinformation andknowledgeexchange Developpingacommonvision Co creatingcrucialartifacts:organicstandard, certification guidelines, etc  Creationoforganisations  irstmeetings Improvementof thegeneralcontext Structuration Commiteescreation Involvementof newactors Expansion of the actionsPreliminarystages irthphase rowthphaseAligningorganisationsamongtheecosystem(internalconstrainingfactors)Aligningtheecosystemwiththeenvironment(externalconstrainingfactors)

Partie III – Chapitre 5

Figure 23: Theoretical framework to study how the hub organisation contributes to emergence of
service ecosystems through alignment of members and orchestration

We  chose  to  conduct  a  processual  analysis  of  the  emergence  through  temporal  bracketing

because  “The  decomposition  of  data  into  successive  adjacent  periods  enables  the  explicit

examination of how actions of one period lead to changes in the context that will affect action in

subsequent  periods”  (Langley,  1999,  p.  703).  Three  of  such  periods  were  identified  during

emergence31  (cf  Figure  24):  preliminary  stages,  birth  of  the  ISSE  and  growth.  For  each  period,

actors,  activities,  roles,  and  links  will  be  described,  as  well  as  coordination  mechanisms

implemented by the hub organisation, factors constraining alignment and how the different layers

influence each other. Evolution in the constraining factors of alignment allow to mark the breaking

points between two successive period.

Chronologies of our three case studies have been established and can be found in the appendices

(Annexe E :, Annexe F :, and Annexe G :) describing ISSE’s actors and their activities in relation with

evolution of the context and the innovation journey of PGS.

31 As we previously indicated emergence is defined as encompassing all stages preceding the ecosystem's
maturity and then, either decline, re-emergence, or dormancy

175

Alignment rchestration   coordination mechanisms ub OrganisationOrg1Org5Org4Org3Org2Service ecosystemOrg1Org5Org4Org3Org2 ub OrganisationEmergence

Partie III – Chapitre 5

Figure  24:  Stages  of  emergence  of  an  Innovation  Support  Services  Ecosystem  (ISSE)  and
dimensions of alignment at meso- and macro-level

Squares in dark grey indicate constraining factors which can be considered as aligned at each stage

4.1

Phase 1: Preliminary stages of emergence

The  first  phase  corresponds  to  a  period  of  continuous  improvement  of  the  context  until  being

favourable enough for the emergence of the ISSE. A crucial accomplishment of this phase is the

creation  of the organisation  which  will  later become the Hub organisation (either an NGO or  a

farmer organisation in our case studies). In this phase, the hub organisation follows its own goals

and supports farmers of the region, thus gaining legitimacy among peers. It is the longest one, its

duration is extremely variable depending on countries (30 years in Burkina Faso and Madagascar,

a decade in Senegal). But duration also depends on what interviewed actors consider important

to create a favourable context for the emergence of the ISSE and how far they go in history. During

this phase, the stakeholders work independently, and encounter problems linked to the scaling

up of their support to agroecology (particularly related to marketing and relations with donors and

lobbying public actors).

Alignment is not yet possible because some constraining factors are not favourable as illustrated

in  Table  16.  The  level  of  innovation  is  medium,  mainly  relates  agroecological  and  organic

production (few innovations are yet observable on marketing modes) and most of all, innovations

are made in silo, without co-adoption from the other stakeholders despite attempts of the hub

organisations to diffuse knowledge through bilateral relationships. There is no agreement yet on

value distribution, the question is not even addressed at this stage. Identity of the ecosystem is

176

Preliminarystages irth rowthMESO LEVEL : ECOS STEMMACRO LEVEL : CONTE T ubOrganisationISSEPublicauthoritiesDemandofend usersInternationaldevelopmentprojectsStrong in uenceModerate exchangesMutualin uence ubOrganisationISSE ubOrganisationPublicauthoritiesDemandofend usersInternationaldevelopmentprojectsPublicauthoritiesDemandofend usersInternationaldevelopmentprojectsInnovationValuedistributionIdentityPowerInnovationValuedistributionIdentityPowerInnovationValuedistributionIdentityPower

Partie III – Chapitre 5

weak, there is no agreement, but actors begin to realise that they share some strong values. Power

is highly scattered; each organisation works in silo and there is no power sharing.

Context is neither favourable nor unfavourable in relation to the institutional framework: on the

one hand public authorities organise workshops on organic agriculture, but on the other hand they

express  their  reservations  regarding  the  capacity  of  organic  agriculture  to  meet  with  the

imperatives of food security in the country as expressed by one interviewee “They're not totally

reassured yet. […] Sometimes they're not convinced that organic production can achieve better

results  than  conventional  production”  (S_PO2).  There  is  no  legal  framework  on  agroecology  or

organic farming at this stage despite of the hub organisation’s advocacy. However, awareness is

rising among the population (on the impacts of pesticides on health and the environment) leading

to  slowly  increasing  demand  as  stated  by  BF_Asso1 “It's  the  demand  on  the  market,  because

these days consumers, particularly in Burkina Faso, have become aware of the need to consume

healthy products”. As illustrated in Figure 24, the main activities are conducted at macro-level and

in this phase, the context has a huge influence on the emerging ecosystem.

Another factor of alignment favourable to emergence is the creation and implementation in the

country  of  new  organisations  dedicated  to  organic  or  agroecological  agriculture  (NGOs,  civil

society  movements,  etc.)  and of  projects  dedicated to farmers’ capability enhancement  which

will  serve  as  a  basis  for  the  implementation  of  PGS.  During  this  period,  there  are  often  some

failures  in  attempts  to  create  ecosystems,  which  are  an  important  favourable  background  to

ground new initiatives as one interviewee told us: “Via this framework, a lot of us knew each other,

had already developed relationships, collaborations. So, we were rather familiar, we were kind of

ready to set off on a new adventure” (BF_NNGO1).

177

Partie III – Chapitre 5
