The transition from the preliminary stages to the birth of an ecosystem can be identified when the

hub organization successfully mobilizes stakeholders to collaborate in creating a more enabling

environment for innovation.

4.2

Phase 2: Birth of the ISSE

The  second  phase, “Birth  of  the  ISSE”,  broadly  represents  the  period  when  actors  gather  and

organise  themselves.  The  beginning  of  this  phase  is  marked  by  the  holding  of  an  event  during

which the stakeholders affirm their motivation to collaborate in order to maximise their action by

“[pooling]  their  so  that  they  can  really  move  forward”  (BF_NNGO1),  “sharing  experiences”

(BF_INGO),  “strengthening  collective  capabilities”

(BF_Cs1)  and  “representing  all  the

stakeholders  and  defending  their  interests”  (S_INGO).  To  achieve  this,  the  necessity  of  a  hub

organisation is expressed because “as long as there isn't someone neutral [...] we're not going to

talk  to  each  other,  so  the  role  we  want  to  give  [it]  is  to  facilitate  the  exchange  between  us”

(BF_Cs1). Neutrality here refers to the necessity for the hub organisation not to be in competition

with other ecosystem members. If perceived as pursuing its own interests, it risks losing trust,

which could hinder the engagement of all stakeholders. During this phase, the main actors of the

178

Partie III – Chapitre 5

ISSE  meet  regularly,  during  workshops  but  also  in  smaller  groups  to  structure  the  initiative,  to

agree on a vision and operational modes. These meetings are considered crucial by the actors we

interviewed, to better understand each other and create trust among members of the ISSE; and

actors feel they are missing when they are too scarce: “We need more times when we can discuss

together, more moments of interaction and sharing” (S_Cs). In our case studies, it is also during

this  period  that  several  documents  are  designed  and  validated  (see  Appendices  Annexe  E  :  to

Annexe  G  :):  i)  the  standard  of  PGS  for  organic  or  agroecological  farming,  ii)  certification

guidelines, iii) the national standard of organic or agroecological farming, iv) control documents,

v)  the  articles  and  rules  of  procedure  of  the  association,  vi)  the  logo  for  the  trademark.  These

objects are crucial for the implementation of the PGS, to explicit what will be allowed or not, who

will  control  that  the  rules  are  followed,  what  will  happen  if  they  are  not,  etc.  But  beyond  this,

elaborating  these  objects  together  allows  members  of  the  ecosystem  to  spend  time  together,

learn  to  know  each  other,  develop  informal  relationships,  clarify  each  other’s  vision,  create

mutual trust, etc. Many things that will be of paramount importance afterwards, to deal with the

tensions and discords which will no doubt arise.

During this phase, activities are mostly undertaken at meso-level (the ecosystem-level, cf Figure

24)  and there are limited interactions  with the macro-level. The ecosystem level  influences  its

environment  by  working  on  documents  which  will  form  the  base  of  the  organic  laws  in  the

countries.  At  this  stage,  there  is  still  no  full  alignment  of  the  stakeholders  (cf  Table  17Erreur !

Source  du  renvoi  introuvable.).  Public  authorities  are  globally  favourable,  they  support  the

initiatives in words, work on the development of a legal framework but with little concrete actions

and  absence  of  financing  as  S_Don  mentioned “They  don't  really  do  much  concrete  action  to

support the sector in terms of subsidies, for example. It's largely neglected, in my opinion”. Few

international  development  projects  are  starting  to  support  the  PGS  initiative  as  expressed  by

BF_Asso1 “It was in 2013 that a number of technical and financial partners accepted, or at least

supported, the CNABio initiative”. The hub organisation, with members of the ISSE advocate to

improve  the  legislative  framework  and  to  build  new  projects  with  international  development

projects.  Demand  of  end-users

is  more  visible:  on  the  hub  organisation’s

initiative,

communication is made about agroecology and organic farming and both farmers and consumers

show interest. The former, to reassure their clients on the quality of their products and find new,

more profitable markets. The latter, interested in consuming healthy food but whose willingness

and ability to pay more for their food remains to be demonstrated.

179

Partie III – Chapitre 5

At  meso-level,  factors  are  mainly  favourable  to  alignment.  The  hub  organisation  diffuse

information  and  knowledge  leading  to  multiple  innovations  with  co-adoption:  organic  inputs

developed by private  firms  and  organic  agriculture  practices  identified  in research  centres  are

promoted  by  NGOs  and  farmer  organisations  to  the  farmers  they  support,  new  value  chains

proposed  by  traders  are  adopted  by  farmers,  etc.  The  organic  standard  is  enacted,  which

constitutes an  innovation  in  itself.  The  PGS  begins  to  be  structured,  involving  several  different

actors  in  the  certification  process,  which  is  an  important  innovation  in  a  context  where

stakeholders are used to see foreigners in positions of power (certifiers, state agricultural advisory

technicians, researchers) visiting their farms to judge the quality of their work.

Agreements are found on most aspects of value distribution - economic, social, intellectual and

environmental – thanks to regular meetings organised by the hub organisation and establishment

of rules of conducts to prevent opportunistic behaviours. Economic value is still low at this stage,

except for the farmers and sellers, few stakeholders are getting richer which is not a perceived as

a problem as most actors are non-for-profit organisations. Social value is created related to the

income  improvement  of  farmers,  as  well  as  environmental  value,  but  they  are  not  facing

difficulties linked to the value distribution. Intellectual value is created with the creation of the

PGS, the concept of organic agriculture in a country, the label and associated logo. Tensions are

more visible on this aspect of value with actors reluctant to abandon their own certification facility

(in Senegal) or willing to formalise the value distribution to prevent one actor from taking over all

the intellectual value (in Madagascar) as an employee from the ministry expressed: “our Minister

was sceptical because he was afraid that SYMABIO would take over the organic sector” (M_Min).

Power  is  partly  shared  among  the  stakeholders  and  meetings  are  organised  by  the  hub

organisation  to  take  the  most  important  decisions  for  PGS.  In  PGS  initiatives  there  is  a  strong

commitment to involving all the participants in the elaboration of the label, its functioning and in

decision taking. But participation can be hard to implement on the ground and some countries

encounter difficulties in involving stakeholders (notably the non-farmers) in decision-making.

The last factor, identity, emerges from a shared vision co-developed through a hub organisation

that  facilitates dedicated  meetings.  After  strengthening  farmers'  technical  skills,  organisations

supporting  organic  agriculture  and  agroecology  shift  their  focus  to  commercialisation,

differentiating  these  products  from  conventional  ones  to  enhance  their  value  and  increase

farmers' incomes. This shared vision was clearly cited by interviewees as a factor of success for

the emergence of the ecosystem: “if we really want to succeed in this process, all the members

must  have  a  common  vision  so  that  we  can  really  make  the  PGS  work”  (BF_Asso1).  On  the

180

Partie III – Chapitre 5

contrary, when a divergence in the vision appears among stakeholders like in Madagascar it’s a

reason for not including one actor in the ISSE and it can hinder its development.

181

Partie III – Chapitre 5

An ecosystem  can  be considered  to  transition from the birth phase to the growth phase when

substantial investments are made by international donors through various projects to support the

activities implemented by the ecosystem.

182

Partie III – Chapitre 5

4.3

Phase 3: Growth

The third phase is a scaling up phase which was observable only in the Burkina Faso and Senegal

case studies (in Madagascar, the process is still in the birth phase). During this period and after

consolidating the structure and operational modes of the ISSE, the actors are ready to enrich the

ecosystem with new members. They also intensify their level of activities, increasing the number

of  certified  farmers  and  the  geographical  area  they  cover.  This  is  possible  because  their

structuration and the previous projects that they had, are important guarantees for international

donors  and  development  projects  allowing  for  new  projects  and  funding.  In  this  phase,  the

ecosystem increases its relations with its environment and influence it (cf Figure 24).

In this phase, all alignment factors coalesce (cf Table 18). Level of innovation remains high with

multiple co-adoption on both production and marketing of agroecological and organic products.

Stakeholders agree on most aspects of value distribution, there is a shared idea that the cake is

much bigger than what they can eat, with increasing demand on the market as mentioned one

interviewee:  “Production  is  not  sufficient  to  cover  the  demand”  (BF_INGO)  and  occasional

meetings  are  organised  to  discuss  remaining  dissensions.  The  problems  of  intellectual  value

distribution, mentioned in the previous stage are in the process of being resolved with members

aligning on the “more harmonised, more structured” PGS (S_PO2). The identity of the ecosystem

is stabilised around a common vision allowing the hub organisation to communicate more clearly

toward potential new entrants.  Power  is more centralised than in the previous phase, which is

related to the institutionalisation process that the ecosystem encountered (in general taking the

form  of  a  formal  association).  Important  decisions  are  taken  collectively  through  meetings

organised  by  the  hub  organisation  but  activities are  carried  out  by  a  technical  team  and other

stakeholders are consulted which can lead to frustration amongst members who feel they have

been  deprived  of  their  decision-making  powers  as  a  private  company  director  indicated:

“Unfortunately, that's what's happening at CNABio: the technical team has really created quite

strong leadership for the conduct of activities, and so on” (BF_Priv).

Influence  of public  authorities  is  going in the right direction but with few concrete actions: the

legal framework is set up, but national strategies are not operational yet, no budgets are allocated,

etc. As we mentioned, the most important difference with previous phase, regarding alignment,

comes from the multiple projects and financing, which allow more activities to be undertaken,

more farmers to be supported, in more regions of the country and can also motivate new entrants

(IFOAM,  s. d.).  Finally,  demand  of  end-users  is  higher  than  what  the  PGS  can  provide,  which

guarantees considerable scope for growth in the market for organic and agro-ecological products

183

Partie III – Chapitre 5

as BF_NNGO2 indicated “Today, the market is booming, and I'm not convinced that we can meet

consumer  demand,  especially  in  a  big  city  like  Ouaga”.  But  after  concentrating  on  alternative

markets  (vegetable  baskets,  agroecological  shops,  etc.)  PGS  is  trying  to  meet  new  markets

(supermarkets, hotels and restaurants) and faces new challenges on volumes and regularity of

supply.

5

DISCUSSION

5.1

Enriching the theory of alignment

This article takes a broad view of alignment, focusing on compromise rather than full convergence

among diverse organisations. Facilitated by the hub organisation, collaboration emerges around

a goal that is complementary or collective rather than strictly shared (Strasser et al., 2022; Wegner

et al., 2023). Alignment theory helps explore how organisations pursue a common objective while

maintaining  distinct  roles  and  approaches.  Temporal  bracketing  allowed  us  to  identify  three

distinct phases of emergence of a service ecosystem, characterised by a progressive alignment

of  organisations  regarding  the  different  constraining  factors.  We  draw  on  the  framework
184

Partie III – Chapitre 5

developed  by  Malherbe  and  Tellier  (2022),  which  identifies  six  constraining  factors  shaping

alignment in innovation ecosystems: technology, innovation, value distribution, identity, power,

and  public  authorities.  These  six  factors  served  as  our  analytical  base.  However,  in  our  study,

technological considerations were not retained as a constraining factor, given the limited role of

technological innovation in the emergence of the ecosystems we examined. This does not imply

the  irrelevance  of  the  technological  factor  in  general,  but  rather  its  limited  salience  in  this

particular context.

Importantly, our findings highlight two additional constraining factors – international development

projects and end-user demand – that remain underdeveloped in existing alignment literature yet

play  a  central  role  in  the  alignment  process  in  our  cases.  We  argue  that  these  deserve  to  be

explicitly considered in alignment theory, especially when applied to ecosystems in the Global

South (Alexandre et al., 2022). International development projects exert significant influence on

service ecosystems in the Global South, given their financial weight, externally driven agendas,

and  short  project  cycles.  Despite  their  pervasive  presence,  these  factors  are  rarely  formalized

within  alignment  frameworks,  representing  a  gap  that  we  seek  to  address.  By  incorporating

international development projects as an alignment factor, we extend the theory to better capture

structural constraints specific to aid-dependent contexts.

Similarly,  end-user  demand  constitutes  another  important  but  under-formalised  dimension  in

alignment

literature.  While  demand  features  prominently

in  studies  of  entrepreneurial

ecosystems (Colombelli et al., 2019; Stam, 2015), it has not been clearly integrated into alignment

frameworks as a factor influencing the convergence of ecosystem actors. Our empirical evidence

shows that user awareness and demand – particularly regarding health risks linked to pesticides

–  catalyse  collective  action  and  foster  ecosystem  emergence.  This  context-sensitive  demand,

often fragmented and economically constrained in the Global South, poses unique challenges to

alignment  that  are  overlooked  in  current  models.  By  identifying  these  factors  as  key  to

understanding alignment in underexplored settings, we contribute to enriching alignment theory

both conceptually and empirically. We argue that, while many constraining factors may be broadly

generic,  their  relevance,  intensity,  and  operationalization  are  highly  context-dependent.  Thus,

future research should pay close attention to how these and potentially other factors manifest

and interact in diverse ecosystems.

National contexts across our case studies shape PGS development through external constraints

on organisational alignment. Public authorities play similar roles across countries, with Senegal

standing out for its support of organic inputs. End-user demand is rising everywhere, and while

185

Partie III – Chapitre 5

the longer history of agroecology in Burkina Faso has led to greater awareness among farmers and

consumers,  limited  purchasing  power  in  contexts  such  as Madagascar  constrains  consumers’

ability  to  pay  higher  prices  for  agroecological  products.  International  development  projects

provide consistent support across cases – though this may change in Burkina Faso due to political

instability.

In this paper, we considered the interactions between two  layers (cf  Figure 24): the Innovation

Support Service Ecosystem’s and its environment. This is a new way of considering the alignment

theory by dividing between factors that are internal or external to the ecosystem. These two layers

are in line with the layers proposed by Möller et al (2020), corresponding respectively to the focal

ecosystem (lower meso-layer) and the business field (upper meso-layer). While this framework

considers four layers (the two that we just presented plus a macro-layer and a micro-layer), we

focused only on these two meso-layers in this study. We overlooked the macro-layer because the

evolution of technological paradigms, of the political and economic global, regional, and national

context  that  it  encompasses,  are  unlikely  to  change  over  the  medium  term.  Furthermore,  our

research design was not meant to study the micro-layer.

Building on this layered view, our analysis highlights how external factors can both amplify and

create tensions with internal factors, thereby shaping the alignment process. For example, public

authorities’  policies  can  reinforce  ecosystem  identity  by  providing  institutional  recognition  to

actors engaged in agroecological transitions. However, ambiguous or contradictory policies may

weaken  shared  visions  and  generate  internal  divisions.  Power  dynamics  within  the  ecosystem

may  shift  when  donors  designate  lead  operators,  strengthening  their  influence,  though

participatory  project  governance  can  mitigate  such  asymmetries  between  international  NGOs

and local actors (Alexandre et al., 2022). In this complex interplay, the hub organisation can play

a pivotal role by navigating these tensions and amplifications, facilitating coordination to sustain

alignment despite diverse and sometimes conflicting influences.

An important contribution of this article is also to bring together the concepts of hub organisation

and alignment. While some authors consider that alignment is useful to consider coordination in

a  multiple  leadership  (Aarikka-Stenroos  &  Ritala,  2017;  Malherbe  &  Tellier,  2022,  2023)  with

decentralised management as opposed to a platform approach with a dominating actor creating

complementary opportunities (Jacobides et al., 2018); we propose that alignment theory can help

describe a situation where the ecosystem is animated by a non-dominating hub organisation. The

presence  of  a  hub  organisation,  rather  than  horizontal  coordination,  stems  from  the  unclear

benefits of collaboration during emergence (Ekboir & Cruz, 2012). The hub plays a crucial role in

186

Partie III – Chapitre 5

structuring the ecosystem and fostering engagement until shared governance can develop. The

role  of  the  hub  organisation  is  here,  to  overcome  the  constraining  factors  of  organisations’

alignment allowing the emergence of the ecosystem, in collaboration with the other members of

the ecosystem.

5.2

Discussing the three phases identified for ecosystem emergence

In  the  result  section,  we  underlined  the  different  factors  that  the  hub  organisation  needs  to

overcome depending on the phases of emergence to align the organisations. This is in line with

previous research on entrepreneurial ecosystems, indicating that the role of the hub organisation

– called anchor tenant here – changes over time (Colombelli et al., 2019).

In  the  theoretical  framework,  we  exposed  that  research  on  ecosystem  emergence  failed  to

consider  the  preconditions  necessary  for  emergence,  despite  recognising  the  risk  of  wasting

resources  if,  during  the  early  stages  of  ecosystem  emergence,  insufficient  attention  is  paid  to

fostering  social  relationships,  building  a  common  identity,  and  enhancing  capacity  through

informal interactions (Daymond et al., 2023). In our analysis, we chose to present them as a full-

blown stage of the emergence of the ecosystem because, to be able to support the emergence of

this  kind  of  ecosystem,  it  is  crucial  to  identify  the  precursors  needed  to  make  the  context

favourable. While we could consider that this first phase is quite a static one, when actors wait for

the  context  to  become  favourable  by  itself,  our  results  show  that  two  different  phenomena

coexist: first, the future members of the becoming ecosystem are starting to develop relationships

and trust through different activities external to the ecosystem (which will be key for the second

phase of emergence); and second, the conditioning forces (Möller et al., 2020) are progressively

becoming  more  favourable,  by  means  of  external  actors  or  of  the  members  of  the  future

ecosystem.

During  the  second  phase  of  service  ecosystem  emergence,  actors  gather  around  a  common

objective, they develop a common vision, they start to collaborate, exchange resources (financial,

human  but  also  knowledge-based)  (L.  D.  W.  Thomas  et  al.,  2022),  state  the  functioning  rules

(Koskela-Huotari  et  al.,  2016),  “design  and  implement  coordination  mechanisms  that  [will]

overcome the tensions that exist throughout the project” (Picaud-Bello et al., 2022, p. 453). This

development of  the  social  capital  of  the  ecosystem  is  only  possible because  of  the  trust  built

during phase 1. Even though the ecosystem is meant to remain dynamic (Frow et al., 2019), this

is also the time when the institutionalisation begins and as Lusch et al. (2016, p. 2960) mentioned

“evolution toward at least some stability is part of an institutionalization process in which rules

187

Partie III – Chapitre 5

are  developed  and  shared  and  become  a  vital  coordination  mechanism”.  Structuration  of

ecosystems  is  not  compulsory  but  a  possible  consequence  of  coordinated  action  enabling  or

constraining  the  behaviour  of  actors  (Taillard  et  al.,  2016).  In  this  phase,  we  identified  both

influences from the ecosystem on its environment and from the environment on the ecosystem

which  conforms  with  Möller  et  al.  (2020,  p.  384)  statement  “The  upper  layers  influence  and

condition the activities, choices, and contents of the lower ones; and correspondingly the actors,

ecosystems and institutions of the lower layers construct and constitute the upper ones”. During

this phase, the orchestrator (our hub organization) plays a significant role as it is responsible for

identifying  resources,  negotiating  the  value  proposition  according  to  the  commonly  accepted

vision, identifying participants roles and motivating them with the possible benefits they would

gain in engaging in the ecosystem (Möller et al., 2020; L. D. W. Thomas et al., 2022).

The third phase that we identified corresponds to the second expansion stage of  Thomas et al

(2022)  with  a  rapid  growth,  the  entrance  of  new  organisations  gain  of  external  legitimacy.

Similarities are also found with Möller et al's (2020) stabilization phase presenting an ecosystem

consolidating,  expanding,  and  further  institutionalizing  with  attempts  to  influencing  regulatory

bodies.  Yet,  in  our  case  studies,  the  ecosystems  at  this  stage  cannot  be  considered  fully

functional  and  resilient.  They  continue  to  face  several  challenges,  such  as  securing  adequate

funding  for  their  activities,  managing  conflicts,  regulating  opportunistic  behaviours,  and

demonstrating resilience in the face of external shocks.

Finally, the last stage of the lifecycle identified by Thomas et al (2022) in the business ecosystems

and the three phases of maturity, decline and re-emergence identified by (Cantner et al., 2021) in

the entrepreneurial ecosystems were not found in our case studies. This is due to two different

factors: first the ecosystems we studied are relatively recent and they probably didn’t reach these

phases  yet.  And  second,  Thomas  et  al’s  climatic  phase  “successful  orchestrators  achieving

dominance”  is  characterized  by  orchestrators  aiming  at  dominating  the  ecosystem  to  capture

value  and  gain  legitimacy.  In  the  case  of  the  Innovation  Support  Services  Ecosystems  that  we

considered,  the  Hub  organisation  can  less  benefit  from  dominating  the  ecosystem  than  in

business ecosystems and has less willing to do so. Unlike entrepreneurial ecosystems, ISSE are

formed  around  a  specific  mission.  Once  this  mission  is  fulfilled,  some  actors,  such  as

development  projects  or  international  donors,  may  leave  the  ecosystem  and  the  territory.  The

remaining organisations may sustain collaboration (ISSE renewal), cease interactions (decline),

or suspend ties with potential reactivation (dormancy).

188

Partie III – Chapitre 5

6

CONCLUSION

This  article  contributes  theoretically  and  empirically  by  enhancing  understanding  of  service

ecosystems' emergence, particularly in contexts where national agricultural innovation systems

are not fully developed. It describes the pivotal role of the hub organisation not only in overcoming

constraining factors to organisations’ alignment but also  in mitigating the influence of external

factors  on  internal  ones  by  navigating  tensions  and  facilitating  sustained  coordination.  The

research  underscores  the  significance  of  creating  a  conducive  environment  for  ecosystem

emergence,  including  the  presence  of  collaborating  actors,  available  funding,  supportive  legal

frameworks,  external  legitimacy  of  the  hub  organization,  etc.  When  all  these  conditions  are  in

place,  a  small  group  of  organisations  can  gather  and  start  collaborating  before  scaling  up  the

initiative (both the PGS-label and the supporting service ecosystem).

Despite the credibility and confirmability of the research, its generalizability may be limited due

to the small number of case studies. Due to the long journey of both innovation and the service

ecosystem  associated  to  it,  the  case  studies  were  analysed  retrospectively  which  can  have

induced biases and omissions from the interviewees. While difficult to implement, a longitudinal

study  could  provide  more  accurate  data  and  follow  the  evolution  of  the  ecosystems  after  the

growth  phase  which  was  not  possible  with  our  case  studies.  Replication  studies  in  different

sectors and contexts would help validate or refine the findings: replication in the Global South in

another sector would allow to verify the constraining factor of projects and donors for alignment;

replication with service ecosystems in other contexts would allow to double-check our results on

the phases and factors constraining emergence to make our results more transferable to other

contexts.

From  a  theoretical  perspective,  our  analysis  identified  two  constraining  factors  to  enrich  the

theory of alignment and make it more relevant to a diversity of situations. It also contributes to the

literature  on  service  ecosystems  by  deepening  the  understanding  of  their  emergence  phase,

which  we  decomposed  into  three  key  stages:  (1)  the  preliminary  stages  of  emergence,  where

informal coalitions begin to form; (2) the birth of the ISSE, marked by the consolidation of roles,

coordination mechanisms, and shared visions; and (3) the growth phase, in which institutional

arrangements  and  legitimacy  are  stabilised.  By  making  the  dimensions  of  emergence  and

alignment  more  explicit within  the  service  ecosystems  perspective,  our  study  contributes  to  a

more integrative and dynamic understanding of collective action in innovation ecosystems—thus

enriching  existing  ecosystem  research  with  a  fine-grained  view  of  its  structural  and  temporal

dynamics. From a managerial perspective, the research offers insights for various stakeholders.

189

Partie III – Chapitre 5

Practitioners can identify the most important constraining factors to alignment in their situation

and  work  on  identifying  solutions  to  surpass  them.  Governments,  donors,  and  development

projects  can  support  necessary  preconditions  to  enable  ecosystem  emergence.  This  will  help

managing  collective  action  to  support  and  accelerate  a  diversity  of  innovations,  necessary  to

meet the Grand challenges for achieving the SDGs.

sociale en agriculture

Partie III – Chapitre 6

Résumé étendu

MOTIVATION

Dans  l’article  précédent,  nous  avons  établi  que  l’émergence  des  écosystèmes  de  services

support  à  l’innovation  (ESSI)  en  agriculture  suivait  3  phases  distinctes  et  que  des  facteurs

contraignants  internes  et  externes  devaient  être  levés  afin  de  permettre  aux  organisations  de

s’aligner  et  rendre  cette  émergence  possible.  Nous  avons  aussi  mis  en  évidence  le  rôle

fondamental de l’organisation Hub pour lever ces différents facteurs contraignants et permettre

l’alignement.

Nos cas d’études sont des ESSI qui ont la particularité d’être orchestrés par des organisations à

but non-lucratif (OBNL) ce qui est peu décrit dans la littérature scientifique. Les seules mentions

d’orchestration  d’écosystèmes  (d’affaires  ou  de  services)  par  des  OBNL  que  nous  ayons

identifiées dans la littérature, ont porté sur l’orchestration par des universités (Heaton et al., 2019;

Mbitse et al., 2024). Or il est vraisemblable que ces situations présentent des caractéristiques

particulières par  rapport  à  des  firmes  orchestratrices  :  sont  attendus  un  niveau  plus  faible  de

compétition  entre  l’orchestrateur  et  les  membres,  moins  de  comportements  opportunistes  et

une confiance entre les membres plus facile à créer et maintenir.

Le Chapitre 5 : présentait les similitudes observées dans la mise en place des ESSI pouvant être

généralisées.  L’article  présenté  au  Chapitre  6  :  permettra  à  la  fois  de  tester  les  hypothèses

concernant les caractéristiques communes des organisations hub à but non-lucratif, et de capter

les différences qui peuvent exister dans les modes d’émergence entre des organisations hub ONG

et faîtières d’organisations de producteurs.

OBJECTIF

L’objectif de cet article est donc de comprendre les raisons de la diversité de modes d’émergence,

notamment en nous intéressant pour chaque cas d’étude, à la nature des organisations hub (qui

sont toutes des organisations à but non lucratif mais présentent tout de même des différences)

et l’impact que cela a sur les modalités d’émergence de l’écosystème.

POSITIONNEMENT DU CHAPITRE DANS LA PROBLEMATIQUE D’ENSEMBLE

Cet  article  se  positionne  donc  à  l’échelle  de  l’organisation  Hub  pour  analyser  l’influence  qu’a

cette  organisation  dans  l’émergence  de  l’écosystème.  La  Figure  25  ci-dessous  illustre  son

positionnement dans le design général de nos travaux de thèse.

192

Partie III – Chapitre 6

RESUME (EN FRANÇAIS)

Les innovations systémiques à impact social sont essentielles pour relever les défis complexes

de l’agriculture en Afrique subsaharienne, mais leur mise en œuvre nécessite une coordination

entre  plusieurs  parties  prenantes  et  des  mécanismes  d’accompagnement  structurés.  Les

écosystèmes  de  services  sont  apparus  comme  des  catalyseurs  clés  de  ces  innovations,

rassemblant  divers  acteurs  pour  fournir  un  soutien  technique,  financier  et  institutionnel.

Cependant, ces écosystèmes nécessitent une orchestration pour fonctionner efficacement, et la

nature de l’organisation centrale — l’entité responsable de la coordination — joue un rôle crucial,

mais  encore  peu  exploré,  dans  l’élaboration  de  la  gouvernance  et  de  la  performance  de

l’écosystème.

Cette étude examine quels types d’organisations émergent en tant que hub dans les écosystèmes

de services soutenant les innovations sociales systémiques dans l’agriculture et comment leurs

caractéristiques organisationnelles influencent le fonctionnement de l’écosystème. Grâce à une

étude de cas comparative de trois écosystèmes au Burkina Faso, au Sénégal et à Madagascar,

nous identifions cinq mécanismes générateurs qui façonnent la gouvernance de l’écosystème :

la  nature  du  hub,  l’alignement  de  la  vision,  le  style  de  leadership,  la  neutralisation  de  la

concurrence  et

la  stabilisation  des  relations.  Nos  résultats  suggèrent  que

la  nature

organisationnelle du hub affecte de manière significative l’inclusion des acteurs, la dynamique

de collaboration et la durabilité à long terme, avec des différences émergeant des structures de

gouvernance,  des  stratégies  de  coordination  et  des  processus  d’engagement  des  parties

prenantes.

En  dépassant  les  perspectives  de  l’orchestration de  l’écosystème  par  l’entreprise,  cette  étude

contribue à la théorie de l’écosystème en démontrant comment les organisations hubs ayant des

logiques  organisationnelles  différentes  structurent  les  collaborations  multiacteurs.  Elle  offre

également  des  perspectives  pratiques  aux  décideurs  politiques,  aux  bailleurs  de  fonds  et  aux

intermédiaires  en  innovation  sur  la  manière  de  concevoir  et  de  soutenir  les  écosystèmes  de

services qui favorisent efficacement l’innovation systémique à impact social dans l’agriculture.

Les  recherches  futures  devraient  explorer  davantage  la  durabilité  à  long  terme  et  les  modèles

financiers des écosystèmes orchestrés par des organisations à but non lucratif afin d’améliorer

leur résilience et leur impact.

194

Partie III – Chapitre 6

ABSTRACT (EN ANGLAIS)

Systemic social innovations are essential for addressing the complex challenges of sub-Saharan

African  agriculture,  yet  their  implementation  requires  multi-stakeholder  coordination  and

structured  support  mechanisms.  Service  ecosystems  have  emerged  as  key  enablers  of  such

innovations,  bringing  together  diverse  actors  to  provide  technical,  financial,  and  institutional

support. However, these ecosystems require orchestration to function effectively, and the nature

of  the  hub  organisation – the  entity  responsible  for  coordination – plays  a  crucial  yet

underexplored role in shaping ecosystem governance and performance.

This  study  examines  which  types  of  organisations  emerge  as  hubs  in  service  ecosystems

supporting systemic social innovations in agriculture and how their organisational characteristics

influence  ecosystem  functioning.  Through  a  comparative  case  study  of  three  ecosystems  in

Burkina  Faso,  Senegal,  and  Madagascar,  we  identify  five  generative  mechanisms  that  shape

ecosystem  governance:  the  nature  of  the  hub,  vision  alignment,  leadership  style,  coopetition

neutralisation, and relationship stabilisation. Our findings suggest that the organisational nature

of  the  hub  significantly  affects  actor  inclusion,  collaboration  dynamics,  and  long-term

sustainability,  with  differences  emerging  based  on  governance  structures,  coordination

strategies, and stakeholder engagement processes.

By moving beyond firm-led perspectives on ecosystem orchestration, this study contributes to

ecosystem theory by demonstrating how hubs with different organisational logics structure multi-

actor  collaborations.  It  also  offers  practical  insights  for  policymakers,  funders,  and  innovation

intermediaries on how to design and support service ecosystems that effectively foster systemic

social

innovation

in  agriculture.  Future  research  should  further  explore  the  long-term

sustainability and financial models of nonprofit-led ecosystems to enhance their resilience and

impact.

195

Partie III – Chapitre 6

Article

1

INTRODUCTION

Agriculture  in  sub-Saharan  Africa  faces  complex,  interrelated  challenges,  including  climate

change,  soil  degradation,  food  insecurity,  and  fragmented  market  access.  Addressing  these

challenges requires systemic, multi-stakeholder collaboration rather than isolated technological

solutions (Ferraro et al., 2015; George et al., 2016). In this context, socially impactful systemic

innovations – such  as  sustainable  certification  systems,  agroecological  transitions,  and

cooperative farming models – are increasingly recognised as essential for fostering resilient and

inclusive  agricultural  transformations.  However,  implementing  these  innovations  is  inherently

complex,  as  it  involves  diverse  actors  with  often  divergent  objectives  and  incentives.  Effective

support  mechanisms  are  therefore  needed  to  facilitate  collaboration,  manage  tensions,  and

provide tailored resources for innovators.

To  support  these  innovations,  organisations  increasingly  coalesce  into  service  ecosystems –

 dynamic  multi-actor  arrangements  designed  to  provide  financial,  technical,  and  institutional

support to innovators (Orbell, Toillier, Mignon, et al., 2024). These ecosystems of support bring

together farmer organisations, NGOs, donors, research institutions, and market intermediaries to

create a structured yet flexible environment for innovation development and diffusion. However,

for  these  ecosystems  to  emerge,  function,  and  sustain  themselves  over  time,  they  require

orchestration – a process in which a central organisation, or hub, coordinates interactions, aligns

incentives,  and  ensures  ecosystem  stability  (Autio  &  Levie,  2017;  Bonomi  Santos  et  al.,  2023;

Colombo et al., 2019; Orbell et al., non publiéb; Thomas et al., 2022).

A growing body of research has examined the roles and governance structures of orchestrators

within innovation ecosystems, focusing on how they mobilise resources, align stakeholders, and

sustain  long-term  ecosystem  viability.  However,  less  attention  has  been  paid  to  how  the

organisational nature of the hub itself influences ecosystem emergence and performance. Two

recent  studies  provide  useful  insights  into  this  issue:  Daymond  et  al.  (2023)  compare  the

effectiveness of private versus public hubs, while Mbitse et al. (2024) examine how universities

act as orchestrators in innovation ecosystems. These studies suggest that hub organisations may

adopt  different  coordination  approaches depending  on  their  organisational  structure,  mission,

and resource model. Yet, in the case of ecosystems supporting systemic social  innovations in

196

Partie III – Chapitre 6

sub-Saharan  agriculture,  the  types  of  organisations  that  emerge  as  hubs – and  how  their

characteristics shape ecosystem governance – remain underexplored.

This  study  aims  to  fill  this  gap  by  investigating  which  types  of  organisations  act  as  hubs  in

ecosystems supporting systemic social agricultural innovations in sub-Saharan Africa and how

their  organisational  nature  influences  ecosystem  governance  and  performance.  Specifically,  it

examines: (i) the organisational configurations that enable an entity to take on the role of a hub;

(ii)  the  governance  mechanisms and  coordination  strategies adopted by  different types of  hub

organisations and (iii) the impact of hub characteristics on ecosystem sustainability, stakeholder

inclusion, and innovation diffusion.

By addressing these questions, this study contributes to ecosystem theory. It expands the scope

beyond  firm-led  orchestration,  to  innovation  governance,  by  identifying  the  mechanisms  that

shape ecosystem performance. It also provides insights into ecosystem orchestration in a sector

with significant social and environmental implications.

The  paper  is  structured  as  follows:  the theoretical  framework  reviews  existing  perspectives on

ecosystem governance and hub organisations, presenting the critical realist perspective of the

article. The methodology details the comparative case study approach implemented. The results

section identify key generative mechanisms shaping ecosystem performance. And the discussion

reflects on the theoretical and practical implications of the findings.

2

THEORETICAL FRAMEWORK

2.1

Service ecosystems and hub organisations

Management  literature  has  long  explored  inter-organisational  arrangements,  distinguishing

between  different  structural  configurations,  such  as  bilateral  relations,  networks,  meta-

organisations,  ecosystems,  and  communities.  Researchers  have  sought  to  understand  the

factors  facilitating  or  constraining  these  collaborations,  including  geographical  proximity

(clusters, industrial districts, and competitiveness poles) (de Rochambeau et al., 2008; Gosse &

Sprimont, 2010; Marshall, 1890; Porter, 1998), sectoral and technological proximity (Mbitse et al.,

2024), and the influence of public policy support (Gosse & Sprimont, 2010). These studies have

also examined governance models and contrasting contractual arrangements, such as alliances

with  more

informal  and  non-contractual  relationships  characteristic  of  networks  and

ecosystems.  Another  key  dimension  investigated  is  the  interplay  between  competition  and

197

Partie III – Chapitre 6

cooperation – often  conceptualised  as  coopetition – as  well  as  the  broader  life  cycles  of  inter-

organisational  collaborations  (Auerswald & Dani, 2017; Cantner  et al., 2021; Colombelli  et  al.,

2019), with particular attention given to the emergence phase (Daymond et al., 2023; Polese et

al., 2021; Taillard et al., 2016).

Across  these  theoretical  perspectives,  a  common  observation  is  the  presence  of  a  central

organisation  playing  a  structuring  role  in  coordinating  and  facilitating  interactions.  While  the

terminology  varies – ranging  from  focal  firms  and  hub  firms  to  catalysing  agents,  lead

organisations,  keystone  organisations,  and  ecosystem  architects  (Daymond  et  al.,  2023;

Dhanaraj & Parkhe, 2006; Ekboir & Cruz, 2012; Grab, 2017; Gulati et al., 2012; Iansiti & Levien,

2004a; Moore, 1993; Provan & Kenis, 2007) – these organisations share key characteristics. They

act as connectors, facilitate knowledge exchange, and often occupy an indispensable position

within  the  network.  However,  substantial  differences  exist  in  their  governance  models  and

motivations.  Some  are  oriented  towards  value  capture,  seeking  to  maximise  their  strategic

advantage, while others – such as public or nonprofit organisations – aim to create and sustain

collective  value,  ensuring  accessibility  and  inclusivity.  Governance  structures  also  vary

significantly, with some hub organisations exerting strong control and power asymmetries, while

others adopt more decentralised and participatory approaches.

The  concept  of  a  ‘hub  firm’  as  defined  by  Dhanaraj  &  Parkhe  (2006)  aligns  closely  with

orchestrating  entities  observed  in  various  sectors.  However,  in  the  context  of  agricultural

innovation  in  the  Global  South,  hubs  are  not  necessarily  firms  but  may  also  be  nonprofit

organisations,  leading  to  the  adoption  of  the  broader  term  ‘hub  organisation’.  While  different

theoretical frameworks provide valuable insights into the role of such entities, this study primarily

draws on ecosystem theory, which offers a more dynamic and relational perspective compared

to traditional innovation systems (Mbitse et al., 2024; Ritala & Almpanopoulou, 2017) or network-

based approaches (Frow et al., 2016).

Ecosystem theory, grounded in a biological metaphor, has given rise to multiple sub-concepts,

including  business  ecosystems  (Moore,  1993;  Valkokari,  2015),  entrepreneurial  ecosystems

(Stam  &  Spigel,  2016),  service  ecosystems  (Lütjen  et  al.,  2019;  Vargo  et  al.,  2008),  innovation

ecosystems (Baldwin et al., 2024; Ritala & Almpanopoulou, 2017), and entrepreneurial support

ecosystems  (Spigel,  2016;  Theodoraki,  2020).  These  frameworks  differ  in  their  emphasis  on

market  dynamics,  innovation  processes,  or  institutional  arrangements,  yet  they  share  a  core

premise: ecosystems consist of interdependent actors collaborating – often informally – towards

shared  or  complementary  goals.  Hoffmann  et  al.  (2018,  p3034)  describe  cooperation  in

198

Partie III – Chapitre 6

ecosystems as organisations ‘pursuing common or at least compatible goals while sharing and

exchanging resources and engaging in joint activities’.

This  research  situates  itself  at  the  intersection  of  these  concepts,  drawing  on  elements  from

different  ecosystem  perspectives  to  analyse  inter-organisational  dynamics  in  agricultural

innovation. Service ecosystems provide a useful lens for understanding the intangible nature of

the support services offered within these systems (Djellal & Gallouj, 2009). Business ecosystem

research highlights the complex coopetition relationships between ecosystem actors (Hoffmann

et al., 2018; Le Roy & Czakon, 2016). Entrepreneurial support ecosystems are relevant in that they

focus on enabling actors, though in this context, these actors are not necessarily entrepreneurs.

Finally, insights from innovation ecosystems contribute to understanding how the characteristics

of the hub organisation influence the emergence and structuring of these ecosystems (Daymond

et al., 2023).

Despite  the  growing  body  of  literature  on  ecosystem  orchestration,  there  remains  a  gap  in

understanding  the  role  of  nonprofit  hub  organisations  (excluding  public  institutions)

in

ecosystems  primarily  composed  of  nonprofit  actors.  Existing  studies  have  largely  focused  on

business-driven ecosystems, where the hub is often a for-profit firm seeking to leverage network

effects  for  competitive  advantage.  This  study  seeks  to  extend  the  literature  by  addressing  the

following  question:  What  types  of  organisations  emerge  as  hubs  in  ecosystems  supporting

systemic social innovations in sub-Saharan agriculture, and how does their organisational nature

influence ecosystem governance and performance?

2.2

Generative mechanisms and critical realism posture

The conceptual foundation of this study is rooted in critical realism, an epistemological stance

that seeks to uncover the underlying causal structures shaping observable phenomena (Bhaskar,

1978;  Danermark  et  al.,  2002).  Unlike  positivist  approaches,  which  often  rely  on  statistical

regularities  to  infer  causality,  or  interpretivist  perspectives,  which  focus  primarily  on  actors’

subjective  meanings,  critical  realism  adopts  a  stratified  ontology  that  distinguishes  between

empirical  observations,  actual  events,  and  deeper  causal  mechanisms  (Sayer,  1992).  This

distinction  allows  for  an  analytical  approach  that  moves  beyond  surface-level  correlations  to

probe  into  the  generative  mechanisms  responsible  for  the  emergence  and  evolution  of  social

structures (Archer, 1995; Fleetwood, 2005).

Generative  mechanisms,  within  the  critical  realist  tradition,  refer  to  the  causal  processes  that

produce  or  inhibit  observed  phenomena  under  specific  contextual  conditions  (Gorski,  2013;
199

Partie III – Chapitre 6

Pawson & Tilley, 2010). These mechanisms are not deterministic laws but contingent tendencies

that  operate  within  particular  social,  institutional,  and  historical  configurations  (Elder-Vass,

2010).  Their  activation  or  inhibition  depends  on  the  interplay  between  agency,  structure,  and

context, making their identification a key objective of empirical inquiry in critical realist research

(Mutch, 2010). This approach is particularly suited for the study of ecosystem dynamics, where

complex  interactions  between  multiple  actors,  governance  structures,  and  socio-economic

environments shape the trajectory of innovation and collaboration.

In the context of this research, the identification of generative mechanisms is conducted through

a retroductive analytical strategy (Blaikie, 2007). Retroduction, a core methodological principle in

critical  realism,  involves  moving  from  observed  effects  to  plausible  underlying  causes,

systematically questioning ‘what must have been true for this outcome to occur?’ (Sayer, 2000).

This  reasoning  process  enables  the  reconstruction  of  causal  pathways  by  identifying  the

necessary  conditions  for  mechanism  activation,  as  well  as  the  constraints  that  may  have

impeded their operation (O’Mahoney & Vincent, 2014).

Empirical  studies  informed  by  critical  realism  have  highlighted  the  relevance  of  generative

mechanisms in explaining organisational and institutional change (Edwards et al., 2014; Leca &

Naccache, 2006). In the field of innovation ecosystems, scholars have increasingly called for an

approach  that  goes  beyond  static  descriptions  of  actor  interactions  to  uncover  the  underlying

dynamics that drive ecosystem formation, stability, and transformation (Autio & Thomas, 2014;

Ritala  & Almpanopoulou,  2017). By applying a  generative mechanism lens,  this study seeks to

explain why some ecosystem hubs successfully orchestrate broad actor inclusion and sustained

collaboration, while others face fragmentation and stagnation.

Adopting  a  critical  realist  posture  in  ecosystem  research  allows  for  a  more  nuanced

understanding of causality in complex social systems. Rather than considering mechanisms as

universally  applicable  laws,  critical  realism  acknowledges  their  context-dependent  nature,

emphasising the importance of historical contingency, structural conditioning, and the dialectical

relationship  between  enabling  and  constraining  factors  (Bhaskar,  2016;  Tsoukas,  2009).  This

study,  therefore,  follows  a  methodological  approach  that  prioritises  the  identification  of

generative mechanisms through empirical inquiry, aiming to contribute to a deeper understanding

of the conditions under which ecosystems of innovation emerge and evolve.

200

Partie III – Chapitre 6

3

METHODOLOGY

This research adopts a multiple case study design, which is well suited to the epistemological

stance  of  critical  realism.  In  this  paradigm,  case  studies  are  not  used  to  generate  law-like

generalisations,  but  to  uncover  causal  mechanisms  that  operate  within  and  across  specific

contexts (Easton, 2010; Wynn & Williams, 2012). The aim is not only to describe what happens,

but to understand what must have been true for a given outcome to occur, through a process of

retroduction that moves from observed events to underlying causal explanations (Bhaskar, 1978;

Danermark  et  al.,  2002).  Case  studies  thus  offer  a  valuable  methodological  approach  for

exploring  how  and  why  mechanisms  are  activated  or  inhibited,  depending  on  contextual

conditions.

We adopt a comparative logic (Garreau, 2020), whereby cases are analysed not as instances of

the same phenomenon accumulating evidence, but as situated configurations allowing for the

identification  of  similarities  and  differences  in  causal  processes.  This  comparative  approach

fosters contrastive reasoning and contributes to theory building by making it possible to examine

how context interacts with structure and agency in shaping outcomes (Sayer, 2000).

Three  case  studies  were  purposefully  selected  to  explore  ecosystems  supporting  the

implementation  of  Participatory  Guarantee  Systems  (PGS)  for  organic  or  agroecological

certification in sub-Saharan Africa. The cases are comparable in that they are located in French-

speaking  countries  with  shared  colonial  legacies,  classified  as  low-income  or  lower-middle

income economies (World Bank, 2024), with generally low Human Development Indices (PNUD,

2024).  All  three  countries  face  major  agricultural  and  environmental  challenges  (e.g.  food

insecurity, desertification) and host a minority but growing movement for organic farming, led by

nonprofit organisations and facing limited institutional support.

3.1

Case selection and overview

This  study  examines  three  innovation  support  service  ecosystems  (ISSEs)  built  around

Participatory Guarantee Systems (PGSs) in sub-Saharan Africa. PGSs have emerged globally as

an alternative to third-party certification, particularly suited to smallholder farmers in contexts

where high certification costs and limited institutional reach pose significant barriers. Promoted

by international movements such as IFOAM, PGSs are based on principles of trust, transparency,

and  collective  responsibility,  and  rely  on  active  participation  from  producers,  consumers,  and

support organisations. In sub-Saharan Africa, where informal economies and community-based

201

Partie III – Chapitre 6

agricultural  practices  prevail,  these  systems  not  only  offer  a  cost-effective  certification

mechanism  but  also  contribute  to  farmer  empowerment  and  market  access  through  locally

adapted governance structures.

The three selected cases – located in Burkina Faso, Senegal, and Madagascar – were chosen for

their comparability in purpose and diversity in ecosystem configuration. All three aim to establish

a  functional  PGS  for  organic  or  agroecological  certification,  supported  by  a  set  of  service

organisations  that  form  an  ecosystem  around the innovation.  They differ  in the  maturity  of  the

ecosystem,  the  type  of  organisation  acting  as  the  initial  hub,  and  the  institutional  and  policy

environment in which they operate.

Burkina Faso’s ecosystem is the most established, initiated by a consortium of NGOs in the early

2010s.  Senegal’s  PGS  was  launched  in  the  mid-2010s  by  a  national  umbrella  organisation  of

organic producers. Madagascar’s initiative, still in its early stages, is led by a federation of farmers’

organisations promoting agroecology. Table 19 below summarises key features of the three cases.

The data required for the writing of this article were collected through a research process that led

to  the production of another  article  (Orbell  et al., non publiéb). A more detailed  version of the

research  protocol  and  data  collection  methods  is  thus  described  in  that  paper.  However,  to

facilitate  the  reading  of  the  present  article,  we  provide  here  a  condensed  version  of  the  data

collection methodology that was followed.

Data were collected between May 2022 and December 2023, through semi-structured interviews

with  actors  from  ecosystems  supporting  PGS.  To  ensure  the  robustness  of  the  results,  we

triangulated the sources of information by conducting an extensive review of available documents

and secondary data on PGS, including reports on the activities carried out, the functioning of the

ecosystem, and the institutional context directly linked to PGS innovation (see Table 20).

203

Partie III – Chapitre 6

Based on the initial analyses, the preliminary findings (including the history of ISSE creation and

key  differences  in  operational  modes)  were  presented  to  some of  the  stakeholders  involved  in

supporting PGS implementation. Initially, only representatives from the hub organisation in each

country were invited, but at the request of participants, representatives from organisations heavily

involved in the ISSE were also included. This feedback process allowed the actors to confirm that

the  case  narratives  produced  by  the  researchers  were  an  accurate  representation  of  their

situations and had not been distorted by researchers’ perceptions or preconceived notions. This

feedback phase addressed both methodological rigour and respect for the participants (Roxani et

al., 2021), who took the time to respond to our questions and expressed a need for information

and  lessons  learned  from  other  countries.  Moreover,  since  the  processes  we  are  studying  are

ongoing, sharing experiences is often beneficial for stakeholders involved.

3.3

Data analysis

The  data  analysis  followed  a  rigorous  and  systematic  approach  consistent  with  critical  realist

epistemology, adopting a retroductive logic to identify the generative mechanisms underpinning

the observed patterns in the case studies. The process involved several interconnected stages,

combining  within-case  analysis  and  cross-case  comparisons  to  systematically  examine  the

activation or inhibition of mechanisms under varying contextual conditions.

204

Partie III – Chapitre 6

First,  all  semi-structured  interviews  were  transcribed  and  verbatim  were  subsequently  coded

using a combination of inductive and deductive techniques. An initial open coding phase allowed

for the identification of emergent themes directly from the empirical material, ensuring sensitivity

to  the  perspectives  of  participants.  This  was  followed  by  an  axial  coding  phase,  where

relationships  between  identified  categories  were  explored  in  alignment  with  the  theoretical

framework  of  ecosystem  orchestration  and  multi-actor  governance.  To  maintain  analytical

coherence and theoretical rigour, a structured coding scheme was iteratively refined, allowing for

the identification of key patterns related to actor inclusion, collaboration dynamics, competition,

and organisational stability.

The analytical process was guided by a retroductive reasoning strategy, central to critical realist

research. Rather than seeking purely descriptive or correlational explanations, the objective was

to  move  beyond  empirical  regularities  and  probe  into  the  underlying  causal  structures

responsible for the observed ecosystemic outcomes. This involved systematically interrogating

the data to determine what must have been true for the observed effects – such as the successful

inclusion of diverse actors or the emergence of tensions within the ecosystem – to have occurred.

This  inferential  process  entailed  the  iterative  generation  and  refinement  of  explanatory

propositions,  ensuring  that  potential  generative  mechanisms  were  not  merely  posited  but

empirically substantiated.

A systematic comparative approach was employed to examine the three case studies, allowing

for  the  identification  of  both  commonalities  and  divergences  in  the  activation  of  mechanisms

across contexts. Within-case analysis provided a detailed understanding of the internal dynamics

of  each  ecosystem,  while  cross-case  comparisons  facilitated  the  identification  of  recurring

mechanisms and the conditions that enabled or constrained their activation. Particular attention

was given to variations in the nature of the hub organisation, leadership styles, pre-existing inter-

organisational relationships, and competitive dynamics, as these elements emerged as critical in

shaping  the  observed  ecosystem  trajectories.  By  contrasting  cases  where  similar  initial

conditions led to different outcomes, or where different conditions produced similar effects, the

analysis sought to refine the explanatory power of the proposed mechanisms.

Throughout the analysis, particular emphasis was placed on the role of contextual conditions in

shaping  the  activation  or  inhibition  of  mechanisms.  Following  a  critical  realist  approach,

mechanisms  were  not  considered  deterministic  but  contingent  on  specific  enabling  or

constraining factors. This required an iterative engagement with both the empirical data and the

theoretical  constructs  underpinning  the  study.  The  process  involved  tracing  the  interactions

205

Partie III – Chapitre 6

between organisational structures, governance configurations, and historical actor relationships

to identify how these elements conditioned the unfolding of ecosystemic processes.

To  enhance  the  robustness  of  the  findings,  several  measures  were  taken  to  ensure  analytical

reliability.  First,  coding  consistency  was  verified  through  repeated  iterations  and  discussions

within the research team to minimise subjective biases. Moreover, alternative explanations were

systematically  considered  and  evaluated  against  the  data,  ensuring  that  the  identified

mechanisms represented the most plausible accounts of the observed ecosystem dynamics. By

adopting this structured and theoretically informed analytical approach, the study moves beyond

a mere descriptive account of ecosystem evolution to uncover the deeper causal structures that

shape the orchestration of innovation-driven ecosystems in the agricultural sector.

4

RESULTS

4.1

Synthetic presentation of the cases

As  introduced  in  the  methodology  section,  this  study  examines  three  service  ecosystems

supporting  the  implementation  of  Participatory  Guarantee  Systems  (PGSs)  in  Burkina  Faso,

Senegal,  and  Madagascar.  While  each  ecosystem  has  developed  in  a  distinct  national  and

organisational context, a key empirical finding emerges from the comparative analysis: in all three

cases, the hub organisation – the entity that initiated, coordinated, and structured the emerging

ecosystem – is  a  nonprofit  organisation.  These  include  NGOs  and  umbrellas  of  producers’

organisations with no direct market interests in the innovations being supported.

This result is significant. While the literature on innovation ecosystems has often emphasised the

role of private firms as orchestrators, especially in business or technology-driven contexts, the

ecosystems observed here revolve around systemic social innovations – initiatives aimed not only

at  improving  agricultural  practices  but  also  at  fostering  local  empowerment,  environmental

sustainability, and more equitable food systems. In such contexts, nonprofit organisations appear

particularly  well  positioned  to  take  on  the  hub  role,  due  to  their  mission-driven  orientation,

legitimacy  among  diverse  stakeholders,  and  absence  of  commercial  self-interest.  These

attributes  allow  them  to  act  as  neutral  coordinators  and  to  build  trust  across  heterogeneous

actors.

206

Partie III – Chapitre 6

While  all  three  cases  share  a  common  objective – supporting  the  development  and

implementation of Participatory Guarantee Systems (PGSs)  through service ecosystems – their

configurations, trajectories, and performance levels differ in several important respects.

In Burkina Faso, the innovation support service ecosystem (ISSE) was initiated by an NGO hub

organisation  with  strong  prior  legitimacy  and  experience  in  agroecological  support.  From  the

outset,  the  process  involved  a  wide  diversity  of  stakeholders,  including  farmer  organisations,

NGOs,  technical  experts,  and  market actors.  The hub  adopted  a  facilitative  and decentralised

leadership  style,  allowing  for  collective  decision-making  and  the  gradual  redistribution  of

responsibilities.  Actor  turnover  has  remained  low,  facilitating  the  stabilisation  of  inter-

organisational relationships. The ecosystem exhibits low levels of internal conflict, and the PGS

is  fully  operational,  with  a  registered  trademark  (BioSPG)  and  the  emergence  of  secondary

innovations, such as the development of local bio-inputs and farmer-led experimentation.

In Senegal, the ISSE was driven by a national umbrella of organic farmers’ organisations. The hub

initially focused on structuring the PGS internally with its farmer base before attempting to engage

a wider range of stakeholders. As a result, initial actor diversity was limited, and efforts to expand

inclusiveness  later  encountered  resistance.  The  leadership  style  remained  hierarchical  and

centralised, reflecting the organisational culture of the hub. While the PGS has reached functional

status  and  is  used  in  organic  markets  (under  the  BioSenegal  label),  tensions  persist  among

ecosystem members, particularly around the inclusion of non-farmer actors and the competition

between  existing  organic  labels.  Actor  turnover  is  moderate,  and  secondary  innovations  have

emerged only to a limited extent.

In Madagascar, the PGS initiative is more recent and remains in an emergent phase. It was initiated

by  an  umbrella  organisation  of  farmer  groups  promoting  agroecology,  but  the  process  has

struggled to consolidate into a functioning ecosystem. Actor diversity remains low, and attempts

to broaden participation have been constrained by strong internal divisions and conflicting visions

among  potential  stakeholders.  Leadership  remains  centralised,  with  limited  participatory

governance. The ecosystem is characterised by high levels of coopetition, particularly between

organisations  that  previously  competed  for  international  funding,  which  has  created  lasting

mistrust. High turnover within organisations further hinders relationship building. The PGS has yet

to reach operational status, and no significant secondary innovations have emerged thus far.

These  differences  are  summarised  in  Table  21  and  point  to  important  variations  in  how

ecosystems  emerge  and  function depending  on  the  initial  configuration,  leadership  approach,

actor dynamics, and organisational stability. In the following section, we explore these differences

207

Partie III – Chapitre 6

through a retroductive analysis that identifies the generative mechanisms that shape ecosystem

trajectories.

4.2

Identification of generative mechanisms

The analysis of the three cases reveals significant variations in the functioning of the ecosystems

supporting participatory guarantee system (PGS) implementation, particularly in terms of actor

inclusion, collaboration dynamics, and the emergence of secondary innovations. While Burkina

Faso exhibits a well-functioning, inclusive ecosystem that has facilitated both the development

of the PGS and additional innovations, the ecosystems in Senegal and Madagascar face structural

tensions, limited stakeholder engagement, and constrained innovation potential. To understand

these divergent outcomes, five key generative mechanisms have been identified: the nature of the

hub,  vision  as  an  enabler  of  cohesion,  leadership  style,  coopetition  neutralisation,  and

relationship  stabilisation.  Each  of  these  mechanisms  is  examined  through  a  retroductive

approach,  identifying  the  necessary  conditions  for  the  observed  effects  and  linking  them  to

specific contextual elements (synthesised in Table 22).

The first generative mechanism, the nature of the hub, is crucial in explaining the extent of actor

inclusion.  The  observed  effect  in  Burkina  Faso  is  a  high  level  of  stakeholder  participation,

encompassing  NGOs,  farmer  organisations,  consumer  groups,  and  private  sector  actors,

facilitating alignment and fostering trust. A plausible generative mechanism to explain this broad

inclusion is the organisational  nature of the hub itself. In Burkina  Faso, the  hub is an NGO, an

entity  with  a  mission-driven  approach  and  prior  experience  in  multi-actor  governance.  This

contrasts with the cases of Senegal and Madagascar, where the hubs are umbrellas of farmers’

208

Partie III – Chapitre 6

organisations, whose primary allegiance is to their member farmers, as was mentioned by one

interviewee:  ‘Fenab  initially  decided  to  test  the  initiative,  which  was  innovative  in  the  Nyayes

region. Given that we’re in the testing phase, we couldn’t open up to other people from outside.’

(S_PO2).

Another key condition that influences the activation of this mechanism is the initial organisational

proximity between the hub and potential ecosystem members. In Burkina Faso, the hub had the

opportunity to collaborate with other NGOs (Autre Terre, CEAS, APIL and Helvetas) that shared

similar objectives and governance principles. This organisational alignment facilitated early trust-

building  and  coordination,  allowing  for  broad  actor  inclusion.  In  contrast,  in  Senegal  and

Madagascar, the hubs were the only umbrellas for organic farmers’ organisations, meaning that

collaboration had to extend to organisations with significantly different structures, priorities, and

decision-making processes. This lack of initial organisational proximity made collaborations more

complex  and,  in  some  cases,  reduced  the  extent  of  multi-stakeholder  engagement.  The

mechanism  is  thus  activated  in  contexts  where  the  hub’s  organisational  nature  aligns  with

principles  of  broad  engagement  and  where  early  organisational  proximity  facilitates  trust  and

collaboration. In contrast, when the hub is more inwardly focused and lacks closely aligned initial

partners, as in Senegal and Madagascar, inclusion remains constrained, and collaborations are

more difficult to establish.

A  key  observed  effect  is  the  variation  in  ecosystem  cohesion  and  stakeholder  engagement.  In

Burkina Faso, early efforts to build a shared vision fostered strong actor inclusion and enabled

secondary innovations beyond the participatory guarantee system (PGS). In contrast, in Senegal

and  Madagascar,  the  absence  of  structured  vision-building  led  to  stakeholder  disengagement,

tensions, and competing agendas, weakening collaboration. A plausible generative mechanism

is vision alignment, where the co-construction of a shared vision enhances ecosystem cohesion.

In Burkina Faso, early structured dialogue and frequent meetings ensured collective ownership of

the vision ‘For 2 years, we held around twenty meetings of this consultation framework, which still

exists today’ (BF_Cs1). Conversely, in Senegal, stakeholders felt excluded from decision-making,

as explained one interviewee, ‘It was the first meeting since the PGS was created where we really

asked for their opinion and what they thought’ (S_Cs), while in Madagascar, conflicting visions on

the future of the PGS (agroecological or organic) persisted, preventing alignment.

The  activation  of  this  mechanism  depends  on  three  conditions:  (1)  the  hub’s  intentionality  in

facilitating  vision  building,  (2)  the  regularity  and  inclusivity  of  meetings,  and  (3)  the  perceived

legitimacy  of  the  process.  In  Burkina  Faso,  these  conditions  were  met,  fostering  trust  and

209

Partie III – Chapitre 6

collaboration. In Senegal and Madagascar, hierarchical structures and institutional fragmentation

hindered vision co-construction, leading to weaker engagement and fragmented governance.

Building upon this, the third generative mechanism, leadership style, accounts for differences in

collaboration  dynamics  and  the  sustainability  of  multi-stakeholder  governance.  The  observed

effect  in  Burkina  Faso  is  a  distributed  governance  structure,  where  the  hub  initially  played  an

active role in aligning actors but later withdrew, allowing other stakeholders to assume leadership

and take ownership of the ecosystem. The plausible mechanism to explain this decentralisation

is facilitative leadership, where the leader fosters early alignment but then deliberately steps back

to enable participatory decision-making. It became a tacit but effective rule established by the

first president: ‘The founding team, all of them, they withdrew after two mandates to leave room

for others’ (BF_Cs1). The empirical indicators of this mechanism’s activation include the leader’s

early convening role followed by a conscious withdrawal, contrasted with the case of Senegal,

where  the  leader  maintained  hierarchical  control,  reinforcing  dependency  and  limiting  the

autonomy of other actors, as mentioned one interviewee: ‘Really, really hierarchical: “You do this,

the others do that, then, at the base, they do this’’ ’ (S_Cs). The context-specific conditions that

facilitated facilitative leadership in Burkina Faso include the hub’s prior experience in multi-actor

governance  and  the  presence  of  organisational  actors  with  similar  collaborative  orientations,

whereas in Senegal, hierarchical leadership styles were reinforced by organisational norms and

historical patterns of authority.

The fourth generative mechanism, coopetition neutralisation, addresses the ability of the hub to

mitigate  tensions  arising  from  historical  competition  among  ecosystem  actors.  The  observed

effect in Burkina Faso is a relatively smooth collaboration, whereas in Senegal and Madagascar,

tensions  persist  due  to  past  competitive  dynamics.  A  plausible  mechanism  to  explain

competition  neutralisation  in  Burkina  Faso  is  the  hub’s  ability  to  act  as  a  neutral  convenor,

fostering trust among actors with potentially conflicting interests. The empirical indicators of this

mechanism  include  the  absence  of  major  historical  conflicts  between  key  organisations  in

Burkina  Faso,  contrasted  with  Madagascar,  where  an  EU-funded  competitive  grant  process

reinforced  antagonism  between  actors  and  Senegal,  where  competing  certification  labels

created friction, as mentioned an interviewee: ‘the interests are divergent too. ENDA said, “we’ve

been working for 20 years on our healthy and sustainable appellation. We don’t want to throw it in

the  bin  just  because  you’re  proposing  another  system  now"  ‘  (S_Don).  The  activation  of  this

mechanism depends on the hub’s perceived neutrality and the presence of shared governance

mechanisms. In Burkina Faso, neutrality was established early, enabling trust building, whereas

210

Partie III – Chapitre 6

in  Senegal  and  Madagascar,  competitive  dynamics  were  deeply  entrenched,

impeding

cooperation.

The  final  mechanism,  relationship  stabilisation,  explains  differences  in  the  continuity  of

collaborations  despite  organisational  turnover.  The  observed  effect  in  Burkina  Faso  is  the

persistence  of  stable,  trust-based  relationships,  while  in  Madagascar,  frequent  turnover  has

disrupted  these  relationships,  leading  to  recurrent  challenges  in  collaboration.  A  plausible

mechanism to explain why relationships remain stable in Burkina Faso but fragile in Madagascar

is  the  preservation  of  organisational  memory,  which  enables  continuity  in  interpersonal  and

institutional  relationships  despite  inevitable  personnel  changes.  The  empirical  evidence

supporting  this  mechanism  includes  low  turnover  rates  in  Burkina  Faso,  where  actors  have

remained engaged over time, contrasted with Madagascar, where frequent leadership and staff

changes  have  necessitated  continuous  relationship  rebuilding  and  training:  “Won’t  the

committee change in a year? And will we have to repeat the training? Who will guarantee that the

committee won’t change? That the person sent by DRAE won’t change?” (M_PO3). The activation

of this mechanism is therefore contingent on team stability and the institutional capacity to retain

organisational  knowledge.  In  Burkina  Faso,  these  conditions  were  met,  fostering  continuity,

whereas  in  Madagascar,  high  turnover  disrupted  relational  stability  and  delayed  ecosystem

consolidation.

These  five  generative  mechanisms – nature  of  the  hub,  vision  as  an  enabler  of  cohesion,

leadership style, coopetition neutralisation, and relationship stabilisation – are not isolated but

deeply interconnected. The nature of the hub influences leadership style, as NGOs may be more

predisposed  to facilitative leadership,  whereas  the  umbrellas of farmers’ organisations tend to

adopt a more hierarchical approach. Leadership style, in turn, shapes both vision alignment and

the  ability  to  neutralise  competition – facilitative  leadership  encourages  early,  inclusive  vision

building,  fostering  cohesion,  while  hierarchical  governance  may  impose  a  vision  that  some

stakeholders do not fully embrace, increasing tensions. The alignment of a shared vision interacts

with the stabilisation of relationships, as it strengthens the commitment and trust of the actors

involved. This helps to counteract the disruptive effects of employee turnover in the organisation.

Finally, relationship stabilisation and coopetition neutralisation are influenced by both leadership

style and vision cohesion, as ecosystems with inclusive leadership and strong vision alignment

tend to manage competitive tensions more effectively and sustain long-term collaboration. The

retroductive analysis thus demonstrates how these mechanisms, in combination, shape actor

211

Partie III – Chapitre 6

inclusion, collaboration, and innovation dynamics across the three cases, providing a structured

explanation for the observed ecosystem variations.
